The Fourth Proof: Rational Power Cannot Be in a Bodily Organ
Proves by disjunction that the rational power cannot reside in a bodily organ like heart or brain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that the rational power cannot reside in a bodily organ, based on a disjunctive proof.

Core Idea
The rational power cannot be in a bodily organ because perception of its own instrument leads to impossible consequences.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from the classical kalam tradition, which uses a logical conditional to demonstrate that the rational power (al-quwwa al-ʿāqila) cannot be located in a bodily organ such as the heart or brain. The argument proceeds by showing that if it were, perception of that organ would either be perpetual or impossible, both of which are false. This refines our understanding of the immaterial nature of the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'.
The argument is concise; each part is important.
2
Identify the conditional and its consequent
The passage states: If the rational power were in a bodily organ, then it would either always perceive that organ or never perceive it. Identify these two disjuncts.
The consequent is a disjunction (either ... or ...).
3
Understand the definition of perception
Perception is defined as the obtaining of the quiddity of the known thing in the knower. This definition is crucial for refuting the possibilities.
Perception does not require bodily contact.
4
See why both disjuncts fail
The second disjunct (perception as a form in the power) is declared impossible. The first disjunct (perception as identity with the instrument) is also impossible by implication, making the consequent false.
Thus the antecedent (that the power is in a bodily organ) must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قالوا : لو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في آلة جسمانية من قلب أو دماغ ، لوجب أن تكون القوة العاقلة دائمة الإدراك لتلك الآلة ، أو ممتنعة الإدراك لها مطلقا . والتالي باطل . فالمقدم مثله باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن الإدراك عبارة عن حصول ماهية المعلوم في العالم، فلو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في مادة جسمانية، لكان إدراك القوة العاقلة لتلك الآلة إما أن يكون عين وجود تلك الآلة ، وإما أن يكون عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لتلك الآلة في القوة العاقلة . وهذا القسم الثاني : محال
The Fourth Proof: They said: If the rational power were lodged in a bodily instrument like the heart or brain, then the rational power would necessarily be either perpetually aware of that instrument or absolutely incapable of perceiving it. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: Perception is the obtaining of the quiddity of the known thing in the knower. So if the rational power were lodged in bodily matter, then the perception of that instrument by the rational power would either be the very existence of that instrument, or it would be the obtaining of a form equivalent to that instrument in the rational power. The second division is impossible.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the rational power cannot reside in a bodily organ. The argument uses a logical conditional: If the rational power were in a bodily instrument, then it would either always perceive that instrument or never perceive it. Both possibilities are false, so the premise is false. The explanation: Perception is the presence of the known essence in the knower. If the rational power were in bodily matter, its perception of that instrument would be either identical to the instrument's existence (which is impossible because the subject and object would be identical) or a form of the instrument in the power (which is also impossible because a bodily form in a non-bodily power is contradictory). Thus the second division is impossible, and by implication the first is also impossible, so the consequent is false.

Key Terms
قوة عاقلة
آلة جسمانية
إدراك
ماهية المعلوم
صورة مساوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perception requires some kind of bodily contact or physical impression.
Correction: The passage defines perception as the presence of the known essence in the knower, which does not necessitate material contact. This definition is used to show that even if the power were in a bodily organ, its perception of that organ would not be a physical impression.

Checkpoint
According to the Fourth Proof, what would happen if the rational power were lodged in a bodily organ?
A) It would perceive the organ intermittently.
B) It would either always perceive the organ or never perceive it.
C) It would perceive the organ only during deliberation.
D) It would perceive the organ through a bodily sense.

Key Takeaway
The rational power's independence from bodily organs is proven by showing that its perception of its own instrument would lead to an impossible alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to be a thinking being. But do you ever treat your rational power as if it were merely a function of your brain, subject to chemical whims? What does this proof demand you affirm about your own soul?
?
If your rational power is not bound to a bodily organ, then your ability to know truth is not limited by your physical state. How does that change the way you approach moments of fatigue, illness, or distraction?
?
The proof shows that perception is not a physical imprint. When you read the Quran, do you seek to have the 'quiddity of the known' present in your soul, or do you settle for the sounds and letters? What would it take to truly make the meaning present in you?

Fourth Proof: Rational Soul Not in a Physical Organ
The author proves by conditional disjunction that the rational soul cannot reside in a physical organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional argument that the rational soul cannot reside in a physical organ, and why both possible outcomes of constant or never-perception are impossible.

Core Idea
The rational soul cannot be in a physical organ without entailing impossible perception patterns.

This lesson examines the fourth proof against the rational soul being lodged in a physical organ like the heart or brain. The argument proceeds by showing that if the soul were in such an organ, then perception of that organ would be either always present or always absent—both of which conflict with our actual experience. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Let the full argument sink in before analysing its parts.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the conditional statement
Find the 'if...then...' structure: 'If the rational power were in a physical organ, then it would be either constantly or never perceiving that organ.' This is the backbone of the proof.
The author calls this the 'shartiyya' (conditional).
3
Analyse the two options for perception
The proof splits perception into two possibilities: either it is the mere presence of the organ, or it is a form similar to the organ. Trace how each is shown impossible.
The second option leads to two similar forms in one matter, which is impossible.
4
Understand why the consequent is false
The proof says we know by necessity that sometimes we bring the heart/brain to mind and sometimes we don't. So the consequent (always perceiving or never perceiving) is false.
This falsity forces the antecedent (soul in organ) to be false.
5
Consider the objection and the author's reply
The author pre-empts an objection about the definition of perception and states that the form-based account is already refuted. Even if accepted, the form is a condition, but the earlier reasoning still holds.
The objection is not fully explained here but references earlier proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : قالوا : لو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في آلة جسمانية من قلب أو دماغ ، لوجب أن تكون القوة العاقلة دائمة الإدراك لتلك الآلة ، أو ممتنعة الإدراك لها مطلقا . والتالي باطل فالمقدم مثله باطل . بيان الشرطية : أن الإدراك عبارة عن حصول ماهية المعلوم في العالم، فلو كانت القوة العاقلة حالة في مادة جسمانية، لكان إدراك القوة العاقلة لتلك الآلة إما أن يكون عين وجود تلك الآلة ، وإما أن يكون عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لتلك الآلة في القوة العاقلة . وهذا القسم الثاني : محال لأن تلك الصورة إذا حلت في القوة العاقلة الحالة في تلك الآلة ، فحينئذ يلزم كون تلك الصورة حالة في تلك الآلة ، فيلزم : اجتماع صورتين متماثلتين في تلك المادة ، والجمع بين المثلين: محال ، ولما بطل هذا القسم الأول ، وهو أن يقال : إن القوة العاقلة ، لو أدركت آلتها لكان إدراكها لتلك الآلة عبارة عن نفس حضور تلك الآلة عند القوة العاقلة . وهذا القدر من الحضور ، إن كان كافياً في حصول الإدراك وجب حصول الإدراك دائماً . وإن لم يكن كافياً ، امتنع حصول الإدراك في شيء من الأوقات . لأنه لو حصل في بعض الأوقات، لكان ذلك بسبب أمر زائد على مجرد حضور صورة الألة ، وذلك الزائد يجب أن يكون عبارة عن حصول صورة مساوية لها في القوة الناطقة ، بناء على القاعدة المشهورة من أن إدراك الشيء عبارة عن حضور صورة مساوية للمعقول في العاقل : وقد بينا أن ذلك محال . فثبت بهذا البيان : أن القوة العاقلة لو كانت حالة في جسم ، كقلب أو دماغ ، لوجب أن تكون مدركة لذلك الجسم دائماً ، أو تكون ممتنعة الإدراك دائمًا . أما بيان أن هذا التالي محال : فلانا نعلم بالضرورة : أنا في بعض الأوقات نستحضر صورة القلب والدماغ في عقولنا ، وفي بعض الأوقات تكون غافلين عن هذا الإستحضار . فثبت بما ذكرنا : صحة الشرطية ، وثبت : فساد التالي ، فيلزم فساد المقدم . والإعتراض : إنا قد ذكرنا الدلائل الكثيرة على أن حصول صورة مسلوبة في تمام الماهية للمعقول في جوهر العاقل محال ، فلا نعيد تلك الدلائل . ثم لئن سلمنا ذلك ، لكن حصول تلك الصورة ، يكون شرطاً لحصول الإدراك.
The fourth proof: They said: If the rational power were located in a physical organ, such as the heart or brain, then the rational power would necessarily be either perpetually perceiving that organ or absolutely prevented from perceiving it. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. Explanation of the conditional: Perception is the obtaining of the essence of the known thing in the knower. So if the rational power were located in a material substance, then the perception of that organ by the rational power would either be identical with the existence of that organ, or it would be the obtaining of a form similar to that organ in the rational power. The second division is impossible, because if that form resides in the rational power which is located in that organ, then it would necessitate that form being located in that organ, thus requiring the conjunction of two similar forms in that matter, and the conjunction of similar things is impossible. And when this division is invalidated, the first division remains: that if the rational power perceived its organ, its perception of that organ would be the very presence of that organ with the rational power. If this amount of presence is sufficient for the occurrence of perception, then perception would necessarily occur always. And if it is not sufficient, then the occurrence of perception would be impossible at any time. For if it occurred at some times, that would be due to something additional beyond mere presence of the form of the organ, and that additional thing must be the obtaining of a form similar to it in the rational power, based on the well-known principle that perception of a thing is the presence of a form similar to the intelligible in the knower. And we have shown that this is impossible. Thus it is established by this explanation that if the rational power were located in a body such as the heart or brain, it would necessarily be either always perceiving that body or permanently failing to perceive it. As for the demonstration that this consequent is impossible: because we know by necessity that at some times we bring to mind the form of the heart and brain in our minds, and at some times we are heedless of this recollection. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: the validity of the conditional, and the falsity of the consequent; therefore the falsity of the antecedent follows. The objection: We have indeed mentioned many proofs that the obtaining of a form completely identical in essence to the intelligible in the substance of the knower is impossible, so we will not repeat those proofs. Then even if we concede that, yet the obtaining of that form is a condition for the occurrence of perception.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that the rational power cannot reside in a physical organ such as the heart or brain. The proof is structured as a conditional: if the rational power were in a physical organ, then it would either always perceive that organ or never perceive it at all. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is false. The author then explains the conditional. Perception is the obtaining of the known thing's essence in the knower. If the rational power is in a material substance, then its perception of that organ would either be identical with the existence of that organ, or it would be the acquisition of a form similar to that organ in the rational power. The second option is impossible because that form would reside in the rational power, which is in the organ, leading to two similar forms in the same matter, which is impossible. The first option (that perception is the presence of the organ itself) is then examined. If that presence is sufficient for perception, then perception would be constant. If not sufficient, then perception would never occur. Thus the rational power would either always perceive the organ or never perceive it. But we know by necessity that we sometimes call to mind the form of the heart and brain, and sometimes we are oblivious. So the consequent is false, and thus the antecedent is false. Then the author addresses an objection: he has already provided many proofs that the acquisition of a form completely identical in essence to the intelligible in the substance of the knower is impossible, and he will not repeat them. Even if one accepts that, such a form is a condition for perception.

Key Terms
القوة العاقلة
آلة جسمانية
الإدراك
صورة مساوية
الجمع بين المثلين
الشرطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that perception always requires a form in the knower, so the rational soul could still be in a physical organ if perception is defined differently.
Correction: The proof actually considers both possibilities (presence and form). It shows that even if perception is defined as mere presence, it still leads to impossibility. Hence, no definition of perception saves the claim.

Checkpoint
What is the conditional statement in the fourth proof?
A) If the rational power is in a physical organ, then it perceives the organ constantly.
B) If the rational power is in a physical organ, then it never perceives the organ.
C) If the rational power is in a physical organ, then it either always perceives it or never perceives it.
D) If the rational power is not in a physical organ, then perception is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The rational soul cannot reside in a physical organ because doing so would force a pattern of perception (always or never) that contradicts our actual experience of intermittent awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your rational soul is not confined to your brain or heart, then what is your true self? Are you merely a collection of biological processes, or is there something in you that transcends matter? In what moments today did you act only out of physical desire, forgetting your immaterial essence?
?
This proof shows that your awareness of your own body (like the heart or brain) is intermittent. You are not always thinking of your body. So where is your identity when you are not aware of your body? If your body is not your seat, does that change your attachment to it? Will you cling less to comfort and more to the soul's demands?
?
You experience moments of clear thought and then heedlessness. If the soul were physical, that pattern would be impossible. So every time your mind wanders or returns to focus, you are witnessing a proof of your soul's immateriality. Are you living as if you believe that? When you neglect your afterlife, you act as if your soul were nothing more than neurons. What will you change today to align your life with this truth?

Second Objection: Mental Image Equals Object?
Author presents and refutes the equality objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mental image of a thing cannot be identical to the thing itself, and how this refutes the objection of two similars.

Core Idea
A mental image is not the thing itself—otherwise conceiving fire would burn the mind.

This passage addresses a key objection against the immateriality of the rational soul. The opponent argues that if the mental image were different from the object, then two similars would coexist. The author counters by showing that complete equality is absurd, using three clear examples. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the opponent's challenge, then three arguments, then a conclusion.
The passage uses 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث' to enumerate the arguments.
2
Identify the three proofs
Each proof shows a different absurd consequence of claiming complete equality between mental image and object. List them in your own words.
The proofs involve God, heaven/earth, and fire/snow.
3
Understand the conclusion
After refuting complete equality, the author explains that without equality, the original problem of two similars disappears.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : أن يقال : أتدعون أنا إذا عقلنا شيئاً ، فإن الصورة الحاضرة في العقل مساوية لذلك المعقول من جميع الوجوه والاعتبارات، أو لا توجبون حصول هذه المساواة من كل الوجوه ؟ والأول لا يقوله عاقل . وتقريره من وجوه : الأول : إنا إذا عقلنا الله تعالى وجب أن تكون الصورة الحاصلة في عقولنا : موجوداً واجب الوجود لذاته ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل . الثاني : إنا إذا عقلنا السماء والأرض ، فهذه الصورة العقلية : كيفية قائمة بالنفس جزئية . وهذه الكيفية لا تحس ولا تلمس ، والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنها لا تساوي هذا الفلك في جميع أجزاء الماهية والصفات . الثالث : إنه لو كان الأمر كذلك ، فإذا عقلنا النار ، وجب أن تحصل النار في عقولنا . وإذا عقلنا الثلج ، وجب أن يحصل الثلج في عقولنا ، فتكون النار العقلية ، والثلج العقلي، مساوياً للنار والثلج الموجودين في الخارج ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل . وأما إذا سلمتم : أن الصورة العقلية ، لا يجب أن تكون مساوية لذات المعقول من جميع الوجوه ، فحينئذ لا يلزم من حدوث صورة أخرى في القلب ، أو الدماغ في القوة العاقلة : اجتماع المثلين
The second question: [The opponent] says: Do you claim that when we conceive something, the form present in the intellect is equal to that conceived object in all aspects and considerations, or do you not necessitate the occurrence of this equality in every respect? The first is not said by any rational person. Its elaboration is in several aspects: First: If we conceive God the Exalted, then the form occurring in our intellects would necessarily be an existent necessary of existence by itself, and that is not said by any rational person. Second: If we conceive the heaven and the earth, then this intellectual form is a quality subsisting in the soul, particular. This quality is neither sensed nor touched, and necessary knowledge is obtained that it is not equal to this celestial sphere in all parts of essence and attributes. Third: If the matter were so, then if we conceive fire, fire would necessarily occur in our intellects; and if we conceive snow, snow would necessarily occur in our intellects. Thus the intellectual fire and intellectual snow would be equal to the fire and snow existing externally, and that is not said by any rational person. But if you concede that the intellectual form does not necessarily have to be equal to the essence of the conceived object in all respects, then it does not follow from the occurrence of another form in the heart or the brain in the rational faculty that two similars coexist.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second objection against the claim that the rational soul is a body. The objector asks whether the mental image of a thing is equal to that thing in all respects. The author argues that the first option (complete equality) is absurd, giving three proofs: 1) The concept of God would then be a necessary existent, which is blasphemous. 2) The mental image of the sky is a particular quality, not a physical object. 3) If the mental image were identical to the object, then conceiving fire would produce real fire in the mind. Since these are impossible, the objector must concede inequality, and thus the problem of two similars in the same locus does not arise.

Key Terms
الصورة العقلية
المعقول
اجتماع المثلين
القوة العاقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The mental image of a thing is identical to the thing itself in all respects.
Correction: The passage shows that mental images are not identical; they are intellectual forms with different properties than their objects.

Checkpoint
What is the third argument given against the claim that an intellectual form equals the object in all respects?
A) Conceiving God would make God contingent.
B) Conceiving fire would produce real fire in the mind.
C) The mind cannot conceive large objects.
D) The intellect is always aware of itself.

Key Takeaway
The mental image of a thing is not identical to the thing itself, so no issue of two similars arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you ever treat that mental image as if it were God Himself, limiting Him to your conception? How can you guard against shrinking the Infinite to a picture in your mind?
?
In your daily life, do you react to your own mental images of people or situations as if they were the reality, causing unnecessary fear or anger? What step will you take today to distinguish between your mental image and the actual person?
?
The author says the concept of fire doesn't burn. What 'fire' in your life—anxiety, desire, resentment—do you treat as real and overwhelming, when in fact it is only a mental form? How can you remind yourself of this truth today?

Third Objection: Sufficiency of Distinction in Locus
Objector questions why coexistence of two similars is impossible, given different loci.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection's argument that two similars can coexist if they inhere in different substrates, and why the author addresses this.

Core Idea
Two forms can be distinct even if they coexist, as long as their immediate loci differ.

The author now considers a third objection that challenges the impossibility of two similars coexisting. The objector argues that the rational faculty and the mental image it receives are in different loci—one in the heart, the other in the faculty itself—so they are distinct enough. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow. Note the objector's concessive tone ('Grant that...').
2
Identify the two loci
The rational faculty is in the heart/brain; the mental form is in the rational faculty. Understand how this layered ontology is used.
3
Evaluate the objection
Ask yourself: Why does the objector think this difference is sufficient? What assumption is being challenged?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : هب أنه يلزم اجتماع المثلين . فلم قلتم : إن ذلك محال ؟ وبيانه : أن القوة العاقلة حالة في جوهر القلب أو الدماغ ، وهذه الصورة الحادثة : حالة في القوة العاقلة ، فإحدى الصورتين حالة محل القوة العاقلة ، والثانية حالة فيها . فلم لا يكفي هذا القدر من التفاوت في حصول المغايرة ؟
The third question: Grant that the coexistence of two similars follows. Then why have you said that this is impossible? Its clarification is that the rational faculty is an accident in the substance of the heart or the brain, and this newly occurring form is an accident in the rational faculty. So one of the two forms is an accident of the locus of the rational faculty, and the second is an accident within it. So why is this amount of difference not sufficient for the occurrence of distinction?
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection. The objector grants, for argument's sake, that two similars might coexist, but questions why this is considered impossible. The objection points out that the first form (the rational faculty itself) inheres in the heart or brain, while the second form (the newly occurring image) inheres in the rational faculty. Since their 'where' is different—one is in a substance, the other in an accident—there is sufficient distinction to avoid true identity. Thus the impossibility of two similars does not apply here.

Key Terms
مجتمع المثلين
القوة العاقلة
المحل
المغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two forms in the same overall location are identical.
Correction: They can be distinguished by their immediate substrate (one in a substance, one in an accident).

Checkpoint
How does the objector argue that two forms can be distinct even if they coexist?
A) By their different colors
B) By one being in a substance and the other in an accident
C) By one being mental and the other physical
D) By their different sizes

Key Takeaway
The objection argues that two similars can be distinguished by their immediate loci, so coexistence is not automatically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever hold two beliefs that seem the same but are actually rooted in different 'loci'—e.g., one from tradition and one from personal reasoning? How can you test their distinction without conflating them?
?
The objector uses logic to challenge a theological axiom. When you encounter a logical argument that contradicts a core belief, do you dismiss the belief or reexamine the logic? What would it look like to let both be true?
?
Your heart (qalb) is the locus of your rational faculty. Are you aware of the 'accidents'—thoughts, desires—that inhere in it? Which of those need to be distinguished from each other to avoid spiritual confusion?

The Fourth Objection: Vision and Two Similars
Author presents a dilemma from vision to challenge the impossibility of two similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection from vision challenges the impossibility of two similars and why it presents a dilemma for the opponent.

Core Idea
A dilemma from vision shows that two similars are accepted in sight, so why not in intellect?

In this passage, the author presents a classic reductio argument that uses the analogy of vision to undermine the opponent's claim that two similars cannot coexist. The objection forces the opponent into a dilemma: either vision requires an image in the eye (leading to two similars) or it does not (undermining the opponent's own position). Either way, the objection exposes an inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure of the dilemma: two conditional statements leading to a contradiction.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فإن توقف عليه ... وإن كان إدراك الشيء لا يتوقف'.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Identify that the first horn (if vision requires an image) leads to two similars, and the second horn (if not) invalidates the opponent's argument.
The author uses 'فإذا جاز هناك، فلم لا يجوز مثله في مسألتنا؟' to show inconsistency.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Understand that this objection is part of a larger argument about the nature of the rational soul. The opponent's claim about intellectual perception depends on the analogy with vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : نحن إذا رأينا المسافة الطويلة والامتداد الطويل، فهل يتوقف هذا الإبصار على ارتسام صورة المرئي في عين الرائي ، أو لا يتوقف ؟ فإن توقف عليه ، فحينئذ يلزم اجتماع المثلين. لأن القوة الباصرة عندكم جسمانية ، فهي حالة في محل له حجم ومقدار، فإذا حصل فيه مقدار المرئي وحجميته ، فحينئذ يلزم اجتماع المثلين ، فإذا جاز هناك ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في مسألتنا ؟ وإن كان إدراك الشيء لا يتوقف على حصول صورة المرئي في ، فحينئذ يبطل قولهم : إن إدراك القلب والدماغ يتوقف على حصول صورة القلب والدماغ في القوة العاقلة
The fourth question: When we see a long distance and a long extension, does this vision depend on the imprinting of the image of the seen object in the eye of the seer, or does it not depend? If it depends, then the coexistence of two similars follows. Because the visual faculty according to you is corporeal, so it is an accident in a locus that has volume and magnitude. So if the magnitude and volume of the seen object occur in it, then the coexistence of two similars follows. So if that is permissible there, why is the like of it not permissible in our issue? And if the perception of a thing does not depend on the occurrence of the image of the seen object in [the eye], then their statement is invalidated: that the perception of the heart and brain depends on the occurrence of the image of the heart and brain in the rational faculty.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection, which uses an analogy from vision. The objector asks: does seeing a large object require an image of that object in the eye? If yes, then the eye, being corporeal, would contain both its own size and the size of the seen object, leading to two similars in the same locus. But this is accepted in vision, so why not in intellectual perception? If vision does not require an image, then the opponent's claim that perception of the heart requires an image of the heart is false. Either way, the objection against the corporeality of the rational soul fails.

Correction: The objector uses the possibility of two similars in vision as a reductio ad absurdum, not as an assertion. They argue that if the opponent accepts two similars in vision, they should accept it in intellectual perception too.

Key Takeaway
The objection uses an analogy from vision to show that the impossibility of two similars is not absolute, undermining the opponent's argument against the corporeality of the soul.

?

?
In your pursuit of knowledge, do you ever use hypothetical arguments to avoid committing to a position, as the objector does here? How can you ensure that your reasoning leads you to a firm and honest conviction rather than just clever debate?

Fifth Objection: Perpetual Awareness of the Body
Author refutes the claim that a rational soul in a body would always be aware of that body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two views of the soul's relation to the body and how the objection of perpetual awareness is refuted.

Core Idea
The soul as a body itself avoids the objection of perpetual awareness.

The fifth objection claims that if the rational soul were in a body, it would always perceive that body, but we are not always aware of our heart or brain. Sanusi replies by distinguishing between two views: the soul as an accident in the heart/brain, and the soul as a specific body interwoven with parts of the body. The latter view does not entail perpetual awareness of the body as an object, so the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: objection, distinction, rejoinder, conclusion.
The passage uses dialectical language typical of kalam debates.
2
Identify the two positions on the soul
The author contrasts the view that the soul is an accident in the heart/brain with the view that it is a specific body interwoven with parts. Understand how each relates to the objection.
The first view is vulnerable to the objection; the second is not.
3
Analyze the rejoinder
The objector counters that perpetual awareness of the body would still follow. The author explains that the second view accepts that we are always aware of being that specific body.
This acceptance defuses the objection.
4
Understand the conclusion
The objection is dismissed entirely because it only refutes one specific theory, not the alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : هب أنه يصح لكم بالدليل : أن القوة العاقلة لو كانت حالة في جسم، لوجب أن تكون دائمة الإدراك لذلك الجسم فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر كذلك ؟ قوله : ( لأجل أن إدراكنا بقلبنا ودماغنا ، غير دائم قلنا : فهذا الدليل الذي ذكرتم ، إنما يصح لإبطال قول من يقول : « القوة العاقلة حالة في القلب أو الدماغ ، فأما من يقول : « إنها حالة في جسم مخصوص ، مشابك لبعض بطون القلب ، أو بعض بطون الدماغ ، فهذا الإلزام غير وارد عليه . فإن قالوا : فيلزم أن يكون أبداً عالماً بوجود ذلك الجسم . فنقول : إن من يقول : ( النفس جسم مخصوص موصوف بصفة مخصوصة ) يلتزم ذلك ، ويقول : إن الإنسان أبداً يكون عالماً بأنه جسم مخصوص ، ولا يزول عن عقله ذلك البتة . فسقطت هذه الحجة بالكلية
The fifth question: Grant that it is valid for you by argument: that if the rational faculty were an accident in a body, it would be necessary that it always perceive that body. So why is it not permissible that the matter be so? His saying: 'Because our perception by our heart and brain is not perpetual.' We say: This argument that you mentioned is only valid to invalidate the statement of whoever says: 'The rational faculty is an accident in the heart or the brain.' As for whoever says: 'It is an accident in a specific body, interwoven with some cavities of the heart or some cavities of the brain,' then this objection does not apply to him. For if they say: 'Then it would follow that he is always aware of the existence of that body.' We reply: Whoever says 'The soul is a specific body described with a specific attribute' accepts that, and says that man is always aware that he is a specific body, and that never leaves his mind. So this argument falls entirely.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its refutation. The objector argues that if the rational soul were in a body, it would always be aware of that body, but we are not always aware of our heart or brain. The author responds by distinguishing two views: one says the soul is an accident in the heart/brain (which might entail constant awareness), but another says the soul is a specific body interwoven with parts of the heart/brain. The latter view does not require constant awareness of the body as an object, but rather the soul is itself the body. The proponent of this view accepts that we are always aware of being that specific body, which is plausible. Therefore, the objection does not refute this second view.

Key Terms

الجسم المخصوص
الإدراك الدائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the soul is in the body, it must always perceive that body.
Correction: This only holds if the soul is an accident in the body; if the soul is itself a specific body, then perpetual awareness is of being that body, which is acceptable and does not contradict experience.

Checkpoint

A) He denies that the soul is in the body at all.
B) He distinguishes between the soul as an accident in the heart/brain and the soul as a specific body interwoven with parts.
C) He says constant perception is impossible.
D) He agrees but says it is not a problem.

Key Takeaway
The fifth objection fails because it only targets one specific theory of the soul's relation to the body, not the alternative that the soul is itself a specific body.

The Fifth Proof: Self-Knowledge Implies the Soul is Self-Subsistent
A proof that self-knowledge necessitates the soul's independence from the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that self-knowledge establishes the soul as a self-subsistent substance independent of the body.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is direct presence of self to self, not via an image.

This proof begins with an undeniable fact: every person is aware of themselves. The author, al-Sanusi, uses this self-awareness to argue for the immateriality of the soul. He systematically eliminates the possibility that self-knowledge occurs through an image, showing that only direct presence works, which requires a substance independent of the body.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof in Arabic and translation
Start by reading the full passage in Arabic, then follow the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the logical structure: premise, two alternatives, refutation of one, conclusion.
2
Understand the definition of perception
The proof defines perception as the presence of the quiddity of the known to the knower. This sets the framework for analyzing self-knowledge.
This definition is crucial for the argument.
3
Examine the two alternatives for self-knowledge
The author presents two possibilities: either the self itself is present, or an image of the self is present. Focus on why the second is rejected.
The two reasons against the image hypothesis are the core of the proof.
4
Analyze the two refutations
First, the conjunction of two similars is impossible. Second, it leads to an infinite regress. Understand each argument separately.
The first refutation uses the principle of impossibility of two similars; the second uses circularity and regress.
5
Conclude the soul is self-subsistent
Once the image alternative is invalidated, only direct presence remains. This requires the soul to be independent of a locus, hence a substance.
The conclusion directly answers the question of the soul's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن كل أحد يدرك نفسه ، وقد ثبت أن إدراك الشيء عبارة عن حضور ماهية المعلوم عند العالم ، فإذا علمنا أنفسنا فهذا إما أن يكون لأجل حضور ذواتنا [ الذواتنا (۱) ] أو لأجل حضور صورة مساوية لذواتنا في ذواتنا . والقسم الثاني باطل لوجهين : الأول : إنه يلزم اجتماع المثلين ، وهو محال . لأنه لا يكون أحدهما بالحالية ، والآخر بالمحلية أولى من العكس والثاني : هو إنه ما لم نعرف أن هذه الصورة الحادثة مساوية لذاتنا ، لم تكن معرفة تلك الصورة نافعة في معرفة الذات. لكن علمنا بأن هذه الصورة مساوية لذاتنا . يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بعلمنا بذاتنا . ويعود الكلام فيه، ويمر إلى غير النهاية ، وهو محال . ولما بطل هذا القسم ، ثبت أنه لا معنى لعلمنا بذواتنا ، إلا نفس حضور ذاتنا عند ذاتنا. وهذا إنما يكون إذا كانت ذاتنا [ قائمة (۳) ] بالنفس ، غنية عن المحل ، لأن بتقدير أن يكون في محل ، لم يكن هو حاضراً عند نفسه ، وإنما يكون حاضراً عند ذلك المحل . فثبت : أن النفس عالمة بذاتها، وثبت : أن علمها بنفسها عبارة عن حضور نفسها عند نفسها وثبت : أن هذا المعنى، إنما يحصل إذا كانت النفس قائمة بنفسها ، غنية عن محل تحل فيه فيه . فهذا يقتضي أن تكون القوة العاقلة : جوهراً قائماً بالنفس ، غنياً عن الجسم . وهو المطلوب
The fifth proof: Every person perceives themselves, and it has been established that perception of a thing is the presence of the quiddity of the known to the knower. Therefore, when we know ourselves, this is either due to the presence of our selves [to ourselves] or due to the presence of an image equal to our selves in our selves. The second division is invalid for two reasons: First, it would entail the conjunction of two similars, which is impossible, because one would not be more deserving of being the state (al-ḥāliyya) and the other the locus (al-maḥalliyya) than the reverse. Second, if we do not know that this generated image is equal to our self, then knowledge of that image is not beneficial for knowing the self. But our knowledge that this image is equal to our self must be preceded by our knowledge of our self, and the discourse would return and go on infinitely, which is impossible. When this division is invalidated, it is established that our knowledge of our selves is nothing but the presence of our self to our self. This only occurs if our self is self-subsistent (qā'ima bi-nafsihā), independent of a locus, because if it were in a locus, it would not be present to itself but rather present to that locus. Thus it is established that the soul knows itself, that its knowledge of itself is the presence of itself to itself, and that this meaning only occurs if the soul is self-subsistent, independent of a locus it inhabits. This necessitates that the rational faculty is a substance self-subsistent, independent of the body. This is what was sought.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (burhān) for the immateriality of the soul. The author starts from the undeniable fact of self-perception. He defines perception as the presence of the thing known to the knower. Then he considers two possibilities for self-knowledge: either the very self is present to itself, or an image of the self is present. He refutes the latter with two arguments: first, it would require two similar things (the self and its image) in the same subject, which is impossible because one cannot be the state and the other the locus without circularity; second, it leads to an infinite regress because verifying the image's equality would require prior self-knowledge. Therefore, self-knowledge must be the self's presence to itself. This presence requires that the self be self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsihā), not dependent on a body, because if it were in a body, it would be present to the body, not to itself. Hence the rational soul is a substance independent of the body.

Key Terms
حضور الذات
جوهر قائم بالنفس
اجتماع المثلين
التسلسل
الماهية
المحل
الصورة
الحالية
المحلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge occurs through an image or mental representation of oneself.
Correction: The proof shows that any such image would either lead to an impossible conjunction of two similars or an infinite regress, so self-knowledge must be direct presence.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason the author gives to reject the idea that self-knowledge occurs through an image?
A) It requires an infinite regress.
B) It leads to the conjunction of two similars, which is impossible.
C) The image would be material and the soul is immaterial.
D) It would make self-knowledge dependent on external objects.

Key Takeaway
Self-knowledge is the direct presence of the self to itself, which requires the soul to be a self-subsistent substance independent of the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your self-knowledge is not a mental image but the direct presence of your soul to itself, then your true identity is not your thoughts, feelings, or memories—they are not you. When you stand before Allah in prayer, are you aware of that deeper self that is simply present, or are you distracted by the images and stories of yourself? How can you train yourself to experience that direct presence and use it to anchor your devotion?
?
The proof declares your soul to be self-subsistent, independent of your body. Yet you spend most of your energy serving bodily desires and worldly attachments. If your soul is truly independent and will outlast your body, why do you invest so little in its purification and its relationship with its Creator? What is one change you can make today to prioritize your soul over your body?
?
You cannot know yourself through external means; the proof shows any image leads to infinite regress. So when you seek to know yourself in moments of introspection, do you ever stop and simply attend to the pure act of consciousness—that 'I am' without labeling or judging? Are you willing to set aside the images you have of yourself (successful, failing, pious, sinful) and just be present to your soul as it is before Allah? That raw awareness is the starting point for genuine self-knowledge and spiritual growth.

Objections to the Fifth Proof: Self-Knowledge Theory
Raises multiple objections against the fifth proof, including counterexamples from inanimate objects and animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main objections raised against the fifth proof, including the critique of the perception theory and the counterexamples from inanimate objects and animals.

Core Idea
The fifth proof is not airtight; it relies on contested premises and leads to absurd consequences.

The fifth proof for God's self-knowledge depends on a specific theory of perception and the assumption that a thing is present to itself. This passage presents a powerful critique, challenging those foundations and showing through counterexamples that the proof would imply knowledge in stones and all animal souls. Let's examine each objection carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the structure: it begins by attacking the theory of perception, then adds a second objection about a missing premise, and finally gives counterexamples.
The passage uses 'وأيضاً' to introduce new points.
2
Identify the three objections
Locate the three distinct objections: (1) Perception is a relative state, not presence of an image. (2) The proof depends on an unexamined premise about self-presence. (3) Counterexamples: stones and animals would have to be self-knowing, which is false.
3
Examine the counterexample of inanimate objects
Understand the argument: if a stone is a self-subsistent substance, its essence is present to itself, so it should know itself. The opponent's response about 'stoneness' being a form in matter is refuted by the impossibility of infinite regress of matter.
This shows the proof leads to absurdity.
4
Consider the animal objection
The objector points out that animals perceive themselves, so if perception implies a separate substance, all animal souls would be separate substances, which the philosophers do not accept.
This undermines the universal claim of the proof.
5
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Ask yourself: does this objection completely refute the fifth proof? The objector suggests it does, but consider how a defender might respond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإعتراض عليه : إن هذه الحجة مبنية على أن تعقل الشيء ، يستدعي حضور صورة مساوية في تمام الماهية للمعقول عند العاقل ، وعلى أن التعقل لا معنى له إلا مجرد هذا الحضور . وكل ذلك قد أبطلناه . وبينا : أن الإدراك عبارة عن حالة نسبية إضافية . فإن حصلت لذات الإنسان مع ذاتها ، تلك الإضافة ، صار الإنسان عالماً بذاته المخصوصة ، وإلا بقي [ وأيضاً (۱) ] فهذه الحجة موقوفة على مقدمة ثالثة ، وهي : البحث في كون الشيء حاضراً عند نفسه . وقد بحثنا عن هذه المقدمة عند حكاية دلائل الفلاسفة على كونه تعالى عالماً بذاته المخصوصة . وأيضاً : فهذه الحجة مع ركاكة مقدماتها : منقوضة . وذلك لأنـا إذا أخذنا حجراً أو خشباً . ونقول : إنه جوهر قائم بنفسه ، فذاته حاضرة عند ذاته . فيجب في هذه الجمادات أن تكون عاقلة بذواتها . وإنه محال . فإن قالوا : إن الحجرية صورة قائمة في المادة ، فلا تكون هذه الصورة حاضرة عند نفسها ، بل تكون حاضرة عند مادتها . فنقول : إنه يمتنع أن تكون كل مادة حاصلة في مادة أخرى إلى غير النهاية ، بل لا بد من الإعتراف بحصول جوهر قائم بنفسه في هذا الحجر وهذا الخشب هو المادة الأصلية . وإذا كان كذلك، فتلك المادة تكون قائمة بنفسها ، فتكون ذاته حاضرة عند ذاته ، فوجب كونها عالمة بنفسها ، فيلزم في هذه الجمادات : أن تكون عالمة بنفسها ، شاعرة بذواتها . ومعلوم أنه باطل . وأيضاً : فجميع الحيوانات مدركة لذواتها ، فلو كان الشيء مدركاً لذاته يقتضي أن تكون ذاته جوهراً مجرداً ، لزم أن تكون نفوس الحيوانات أسرها جواهر مجردة . والقوم لا يقولون به . فهذا هو وجه السؤال على هذه الحجة والبيئة
The objection to it: This argument is based on the premise that intellection of a thing requires the presence of an image equal in complete quiddity to the intelligible to the intellect, and that intellection has no meaning other than this presence. All of that we have already invalidated. We have shown that perception is a relational, relative state. If that relation occurs for the human self with itself, then the human becomes knowledgeable of his particular self; otherwise he remains ignorant of it. Also, this argument depends on a third premise, which is the investigation of whether a thing is present to itself. We have already discussed this premise when relating the proofs of the philosophers on His being, exalted, knowing His particular self. Also, this argument, despite the weakness of its premises, is refutable. That is because if we take a stone or wood and say it is a self-subsistent substance, its essence is present to itself, then these inanimate objects must be rational of themselves, which is impossible. If they say that stoneness is a form subsisting in matter, so this form is not present to itself but present to its matter, we say: it is impossible for every matter to be contained in another matter ad infinitum; rather one must admit that there is a self-subsistent substance in this stone and this wood which is the original matter. If so, then that matter is self-subsistent, so its essence is present to itself, so it must be knowing itself, thus these inanimate objects would be knowing themselves, conscious of their essences, which is known to be false. Also, all animals perceive themselves. If a thing's perception of itself necessitates that its essence is a separate substance, then all animal souls would be separate substances, but they [the philosophers] do not say that. This is the point of objection to this argument.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (iʕtirāḍ) to the fifth proof. The objector first challenges the theory of perception used in the proof, claiming that the author has already refuted the idea that intellection requires an image. Instead, perception is a relative relation (ḥāla nisbiyya iḍāfiyya) between the self and itself. Second, the objector points out that the proof depends on an unexamined premise about a thing being present to itself. Third, he offers counterexamples: if self-presence implies self-subsistence, then stones and wood should be knowing themselves if they are self-subsistent; moreover, all animals perceive themselves, so their souls would be separate substances, which the philosophers deny. These objections aim to show that the fifth proof is inconclusive.

Key Terms
حالة نسبية إضافية
النقض
الحاضر عند نفسه
الجمادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fifth proof is logically airtight and unassailable.
Correction: This objection shows that the fifth proof relies on contested premises about perception and self-presence, and leads to absurd consequences like stones knowing themselves and all animal souls being separate substances.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does the objection use to refute the fifth proof?
A) A dream of a sea of mercury.
B) A stone or wood that would have to know itself.
C) The human soul being composed of atoms.
D) The impossibility of self-knowledge altogether.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof is vulnerable to multiple objections, especially counterexamples from inanimate objects and animals showing it leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume you know yourself because you are always present to yourself. But this lesson shows that mere presence does not guarantee true knowledge. In what areas of your life have you mistaken familiarity with your own thoughts for real understanding of your soul? What will you do today to move from surface self-awareness to deeper self-knowledge?
?
The objector argues that even stones would be knowers if presence-to-self were enough. Does this make you question the reliability of your immediate self-awareness? How often do you let your own 'presence to yourself' deceive you into thinking you have no blind spots? Commit to one specific way to uncover a hidden assumption about yourself this week.
?
If perception is a relational state and not mere presence, then your knowledge of yourself depends on how you relate to your own essence. Are you actively working on that relationship, or are you passive? What one habit can you change to make your self-awareness more truthful and less automatic?

Begins the sixth proof attributed to Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, based on imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial premise of Abu al-Barakat's sixth proof, which uses the human ability to imagine fantastical objects.

Core Idea
Imagination can conceive of things that do not exist in reality.

Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi proposes a compelling argument based on the power of imagination. He begins by observing that we can picture impossible or fantastical objects with ease. This observation sets up a critical question about the nature of these mental images—are they real or not? Let's examine the opening of his proof.

The Sixth Argument: Mental Images Prove a Non-Physical Substance
Abu al-Barakat presents the sixth argument for a non-physical substance via mental images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of mental imagery—such as imagining a vast sea of mercury—provides a rational proof for the existence of a non-physical substance (the soul) that is not a body or bodily.

Core Idea
Mental images point to a non-physical substance because a small brain cannot hold vast images.

This lesson presents an ingenious argument attributed to Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi. The argument uses the everyday experience of imagining large objects to establish that there must be a non-physical locus for mental images. It challenges the assumption that all existence is material, and it is a classic proof for the existence of an immaterial soul in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
We start with a simple fact: every one of us can imagine vast objects like a sea of mercury or a mountain of ruby. Acknowledge this as given.
The argument relies on this universal human experience.
2
Follow the logical chain
These mental images must either exist or not. They cannot be non-existent because we perceive and distinguish them, so they exist. They exist in the mind, not outside. Now ask: where do they reside? Their locus must be a body, inhering in a body, or neither. The first two are impossible because the brain is too small for such large forms.
This is a classic 'locus' (maḥall) argument.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since the locus cannot be a body or bodily, it must be a non-physical substance. This is the required result: the soul is a non-physical substance.
The argument does not prove the soul's immortality directly, only its immateriality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : اعلم : أن أبا البركات البغدادي زعم أن الدليل القوي في هذه المسألة ، هو هذا الذي نذكره . فقال : و لا شك في أن الواحد منا يمكنه أن يتخيل بحراً من الزئبق ، وجبلاً من الياقوت ، ويمكنه أن يتخيل شموساً وأقماراً [ كثيرة (۲) ] فهذه الصورة الخيالية ، إما أن تكون معدومة أو تكون موجودة . والأول باطل . لأن قوة الإدراك والفهم تشير إلى كل واحد من تلك الصور ، وتميز بينها وبين غيرها . وربما بلغ ذلك التخيل ، إلى أن يصير كالأمر المشاهد المحسوس ، بسبب قوته. ومعلوم : أن العدم المحض والنفي الصرف ، لا يكون كذلك. فثبت : أن هذه الصور الخيالية : موجودة . ولما علمنا بالضرورة : أنها ليست موجودة في الأعيان ، ثبت أنها موجودة في الأذهان . فتقول : محل هذه الصورة، إما أن يكون جسماً ، أو حالا في الجسم ، أو لا جسم ولا حالاً في الجسم . والقسمان الأولان باطلان . لأن صورة البحر والجبل والشموس والأقمار : صوراً عظيمة ، والدماغ : جسم صغير . وانطباع الصور العظيمة في المحل الصغير : محال في بديهة العقل . ولما بطل ذلك ، ثبت : أن محل هذه الصور الخيالية موجودة ، ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني . وذلك هو المطلوب ، هذا تقرير هذا الدليل
The sixth argument: Know that Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi claimed that the strong proof in this issue is this which we mention. He said: There is no doubt that each one of us can imagine a sea of mercury, and a mountain of ruby, and can imagine suns and moons [many]. So these imaginary forms, either they are non-existent or they exist. The first is false, because the power of perception and understanding points to each one of those forms, and distinguishes between them and others. Perhaps that imagination reaches the point of becoming like a witnessed sensed matter, due to its strength. And it is known that pure non-existence and absolute negation are not like that. So it is established that these imaginary forms exist. And since we know necessarily that they do not exist in external things, it is established that they exist in minds. So we say: The locus of this form is either a body, or inhering in a body, or neither a body nor inhering in a body. The first two divisions are false, because the form of the sea, mountain, suns and moons are great forms, and the brain is a small body. And the impression of great forms in a small locus is impossible by the first principle of reason. And when that is invalidated, it is established that the locus of these imaginary forms exists, not as a body nor as a bodily thing. And that is the required conclusion. This is the exposition of this proof.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dālīl) for the existence of a non-physical substance (the soul) based on the phenomenon of mental imagery. The argument begins by noting that we can imagine vast objects like a sea of mercury, a mountain of ruby, or multiple suns and moons. These mental images either exist or do not exist. They cannot be non-existent because the mind distinguishes them and they can be vivid. Therefore they exist. Since they do not exist in the external world, they exist in the mind. The locus of these images must be either a body, something inhering in a body, or neither. The first two options are impossible because the images are large and the brain is physically small, so large images cannot be impressed in a small brain. Thus the locus must be neither a body nor a bodily thing—a non-physical substance. This is the required conclusion. The proof is attributed to Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi.

Key Terms
الصورة الخيالية
الجوهر المجرد
محل
انطباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental images are physically located in the brain as images impressed on the brain matter.
Correction: The argument explicitly refutes this by noting that the brain is too small to contain vast images, so the locus must be non-physical.

Checkpoint

D) Because the brain is too large

Key Takeaway
The mental image argument establishes that the soul is a non-physical substance by showing that its locus cannot be a body.

First Objection: The Indivisible Soul Cannot Perceive Shape
Raises first objection: abstract soul cannot perceive shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the sixth argument: why an indivisible (abstract) soul cannot perceive shape.

Core Idea
An indivisible soul cannot perceive shape because it cannot distinguish sides.

The author begins a critical analysis of the sixth argument, which claims that perception of imaginary forms requires their impression in a locus. The first objection targets the nature of the soul: if the soul is abstract and indivisible, how can it perceive shape? This passage clearly lays out the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow: premise, consequence, and conclusion.
The passage is incomplete; it only presents the objection, not the answer.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with a premise: 'If the soul is abstracted from corporeality and magnitude, it is impossible for it to be a locus for magnitude and shape.' Why does the author claim this?
3
Follow the reasoning
Trace the steps: abstract → indivisible → same locus for all angles → no distinction between sides → no perception of shape.
This is a chain of logical implications.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that perception of shape is impossible for an abstract soul. Do you see why?
Think about how you perceive a square: your mind needs to distinguish the four corners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذا الوجه ، لا شك أنه أخف مؤنة من الدلائل المتقدمة . لأن هذا الدليل ، مبني على أن إدراك هذه الصور الخيالية ، يستدعي انطباعها في العالم . وهذه المقدمة فيها أبحاث : الأول : إن جوهر النفس إذا كان مجرداً عن الحجمية والمقـدار ، امتنع كونها محلاً للمقدار والشكل . وذلك لأنه إذا كان جوهراً مجرداً عن الحجمية ، والمقدار ، كان [ واحداً(۲) ] مبرأ عن القسمة والتركب ، وإذا كان كذلك ، فالمحل الذي تحل فيه صورة زاوية من زوايا ذلك المتخيل ، هو الذي بعينه الذي تحل فيه صورة الزاوية الثانية من ذلك المتخيل . وإذا كانت الصورة بأسرها مرتسمة في محل واحد ، فحينئذ لا يحصل امتياز جانب عن جانب في ذلك الإدراك ، وإذا لم يحصل هذا الامتياز ، امتنع إدراك (۳) الشكل والأضلاع في الجوانب (4)
And know that this aspect, without doubt, is lighter in burden than the preceding proofs. Because this proof is built upon the premise that the perception of these imaginary forms necessitates their impression in a locus. And this premise contains investigations: First: If the substance of the soul is abstracted from corporeality and magnitude, it is impossible for it to be a locus for magnitude and shape. That is because if it is an essence abstracted from corporeality and magnitude, it is [one] free from division and composition. And if that is so, then the locus in which the image of one angle of that imagined thing inheres is the very same which inheres in the image of the second angle of that imagined thing. And if the entire image is imprinted in one locus, then at that time no distinction of side from side occurs in that perception. And if this distinction does not occur, perception of shape and sides in the directions is impossible.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage begins the author's critical discussion of the sixth argument. The author notes that this argument is lighter than previous ones, but it depends on the premise that perceiving mental images requires their impression in a locus. This premise has several investigations (abḥāth). The first research (al-awwal) is presented here: If the soul's substance is abstract (mujarrad) from corporeality and magnitude, it cannot be a locus for magnitude and shape because it is indivisible. In an indivisible substance, the locus for the image of one angle of an imagined object is the same as for another angle. Since the entire image is in one locus, there can be no distinction between sides in perception. Without distinction, perception of shape and sides is impossible. The objection is cut off here; the answer is not included in this window.

Proof that the Rational Faculty is Not Bodily
Author presents logical and rational proof that the rational faculty is not bodily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses both logical and rational arguments to prove that the rational faculty is independent of the body.

Core Idea
The rational faculty is not bodily because bodily defect does not always cause mental defect.

In this passage, the author presents a rigorous proof that the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-‘aqliyya) is not a bodily entity. He employs two distinct lines of inquiry—one logical, one rational—to demonstrate that the mind can remain perfect even when the body deteriorates. As you read, notice how he anticipates and refutes a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two main sections: logical inquiry and rational inquiry.
2
Identify the two inquiries
The author divides the proof into a logical inquiry (al-bahth al-mantiqi) and a rational inquiry (al-bahth al-'aqli). Locate where each begins.
'Ama al-bahth al-mantiqi' marks the first; 'wa amma al-bahth al-'aqli' marks the second.
3
Examine the logical argument
See how the author uses a conditional: if rational faculty were bodily, then body defect implies mind defect. Since the consequent is false (old man with perfect mind), the antecedent must be false. Note the objection and its rebuttal.
The objection 'defect may occur with old age' is dismissed as 'exception from the consequent' which proves nothing.
4
Examine the rational argument
Here the author argues that if rational faculty were bodily, bodily weakness would never separate from mental weakness. Since separation is observed, the faculty must be independent. Also note the alternative explanation for cases where mental defect accompanies bodily defect.
The soul's preoccupation with repairing the body can distract it from theoretical perfection.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the rational faculty is independent (ghaniyya fi dhatiha) from the body. Consider how this impacts our understanding of the soul and mind-body relationship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطقي ، ونظراً إلى البحث العقلي . أما البحث المنطقي فهو أنا قلنا : إن كانت القوة العقلية بدنية ، لزم من حصول الخلل في البدن ، حصول الخلل في القوة العقلية . وهذا اللزوم غير حاصل ، لأنه قد يبقى الشيخ مع كمال العقل ، فوجب أن لا تكون القوة العقلية بدنية ، لأن انتفاء اللازم يدل على انتفاء الملزوم . أما إذا قلنا : إن هذا الخلل قد يحصل مع الشيخوخة ، فإنه لا ينتج شيئاً لأن الاستثناء من التالي ، لا ينتج شيئاً . وأما البحث العقلي ؛ فهو بينا أنه لو كانت القوة العقلية بدنية ، لوجب عند حصول الخلل في البدن ، أن يحصل الخلل في القوة العقلية ، وأن لا ينفك ضعف البدن عن ضعف العقل . وحيث حصل هذا الانفكاك علمنا : أن القوة العقلية غنية في ذاتها عن البدن . جرم لم يلزم من حصول الخلل في البدن ، حصول الخلل في العقل . وأما حصول الخلل في العقل في بعض أوقات حصول الخلل في البدن ، فهذا لا يدل على كون القوة العقلية بدنية ، لاحتمال أن يكون الموجب لاختلال العقل في هذه الصورة سبيباً آخر وهو أن شدة اهتمام النفس بإصلاح البدن ، يمنعها عن استكمال القوة النظرية . فهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل
The logical [inquiry], and with regard to the rational inquiry. As for the logical inquiry, it is that we said: If the rational faculty were bodily, then the occurrence of defect in the body would necessitate the occurrence of defect in the rational faculty. But this necessitation does not occur, because the old man may remain with perfect reason. So it must be that the rational faculty is not bodily, because the absence of the consequent indicates the absence of the antecedent. But if we say: This defect may occur with old age, then it does not prove anything, because the exception from the consequent does not prove anything. As for the rational inquiry: we showed that if the rational faculty were bodily, then when defect occurs in the body, defect must occur in the rational faculty, and weakness of the body would not be separated from weakness of the mind. But since this separation occurs, we know that the rational faculty is independent in its essence from the body. Therefore, it is not necessary that from the occurrence of defect in the body, defect occurs in the mind. As for the occurrence of defect in the mind at some times when defect occurs in the body, this does not indicate that the rational faculty is bodily, because it is possible that the cause of the mental defect in this case is another cause, namely that the soul's intense preoccupation with repairing the body prevents it from perfecting the theoretical faculty. This completes the discussion on establishing this proof.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the rational faculty (al-quwwa al-'aqliyya) is not bodily. The author presents two lines of inquiry: logical and rational. The logical argument proceeds by conditional: if the rational faculty were bodily, then any defect in the body would necessarily cause a defect in the mind. But we observe that old people often retain perfect mental faculties despite bodily decline. Since the consequent (defect in mind) is absent while the antecedent (bodily defect) is present, the conditional must be false. The author then anticipates a possible objection: one might say that mental defect does sometimes occur in old age. He responds that such a claim is logically invalid—it is an 'exception from the consequent' which proves nothing. Next, the rational argument is presented: if the rational faculty were bodily, then bodily weakness would never be separate from mental weakness. But we observe cases where the body is weak yet the mind remains strong, indicating that the rational faculty is independent (ghaniyya fi dhatiha 'an al-badan). The author further explains that when mental defect does accompany bodily defect, it is not due to the bodily nature of the mind but because the soul's effort to repair the body distracts it from theoretical perfection. This completes the proof.

Key Terms
القوة العقلية
البدن
اللازم
الملزوم
الاستثناء من التالي
البحث المنطقي
البحث العقلي
النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof relies only on one type of reasoning (logical or rational) and ignore the other.
Correction: The author explicitly uses both a logical syllogism and a rational argument to establish the independence of the rational faculty. Both are necessary to address different aspects of the proof.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the fact that an old man can retain perfect reason despite bodily decline?
A) The rational faculty is bodily, but it is resistant to damage.
B) The rational faculty is not bodily, because the absence of the consequent indicates the absence of the antecedent.
C) The rational faculty is independent only in some people.
D) The rational faculty is bodily, but it improves with age.

Key Takeaway
The rational faculty is independent of the body, as shown by the logical and rational arguments that bodily defect does not necessitate mental defect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your body is weak, tired, or sick, do you find your rational thinking and spiritual focus actually diminish? If so, what does that reveal about your reliance on the body versus the independence of your rational faculty? This lesson demands that you identify the real cause: is it the body's effect on the mind, or your soul's preoccupation with fixing the body? What will you change?
?
The author says the soul's intense preoccupation with repairing the body can prevent it from perfecting the theoretical faculty. What 'repairs'—health concerns, physical appearance, comfort—are you allowing to steal your attention from deeper knowledge of God and yourself? How can you reorder your priorities today?
?
If your rational faculty is truly independent of your body, then your worth and your ability to know truth do not depend on your physical state. Do you live as if that is true? Or do you let physical limitations dictate your intellectual and spiritual pursuits? Write down one area where you have let your body's condition limit your pursuit of knowledge, and commit to one action that disregards that limitation this week.

Seven Objections to the Bodily Nature of the Rational Faculty
Seven objections challenge the proof that the rational faculty is non-bodily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven objections raised against the proof that the rational faculty is non-bodily, and how each attempts to show that bodily defects do not necessarily impair the intellect.

Core Idea
Bodily decline does not inevitably cause mental decline, the opponents argue.

Having presented a proof that the rational faculty cannot be bodily, al-Matalib al-Aliyah now lists seven objections from opponents who dispute the link between bodily health and intellectual function. These objections draw on examples from old age, temperament, habit, and even prophetic tradition. Read each objection carefully to see how they try to preserve a bodily basis for the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the first three objections
Notice how objections 1-3 offer alternative explanations: a minimum health requirement, a special organ, and a favorable temperament.
2
Read objections 4-6
These objections appeal to balancing forces, accumulated experience, and ingrained habits to compensate for physical decline.
3
Read the seventh objection
This objection cites a prophetic hadith about greed and hope persisting in old age. The passage is incomplete, but the point is that certain mental qualities endure.
4
Reflect on the cumulative strategy
Consider how each objection tries to break the link between bodily defect and mental defect. They do not deny the body's role but offer ways to account for resilience.

والاعتراض عليه : لا نسلّم إنه لو كانت القوة العقلية بدنية ، لزم اختلالها بسبب حصول كل خلل في البدن . وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : القدر المحتاج إليه من صحة البدن في كمال القوة العقلية : مقدار معين . وأما كمال حال البدن في الصحة فإنه غير معتبر في كمال حال القوة العقلية . وإذا كان هذا محتملا لم يبعد أن يقال : إن ذلك القدر المحتاج إليه باقي إلى آخر الشيخوخة فلا جرم بقي العقل كاملاً إلى آخر الشيخوخة . الثاني : إن الشيخ لعله إنما يمكنه أن يستمر في الإدراكات العقلية على الصحة والكمال ، لأن عقله يتم بعضو من البدن ، يتأخر الفساد والاستحالة إليه ، وإن ظهرت الآفة في سائر القوى والأفعال . الثالث : إنه لا يمتنع أن تكون بعض الأمزجة أوفق لبعض القوى ، فلعل مزاج الشيخ أوفق للقوة العقلية . ولهذا السبب تقوى فيه القوى العقلية . الرابع : إن المزاج إذا كان في غاية القوة والشدة ، كانت سائر القوى قوية فتكون القوى الشهوانية والغضبية قوية جداً ، وقوة هذه القوى ، تمنع العقل من الاستكمال . فإذا حصلت الشيخوخة ، وحصل الضعف ، حصل بسبب الضعف ، ضعف في العقل ، وضعف في هذه القوى المانعة للعقل من الاستكمال ، ويقدر ما حصل من الضعف في العقل ، حصل الضعف في أضداد العقل ، فينجبر النقصان من أحد الجانبين ، بالنقصان الحاصل في المواضع ، فيحصل الاعتدال . الخامس : إن الشيخ حفظ المقدمات الكثيرة ، ومارسها ، وكثرت تجاربه ، وهذه الأحوال تعينه على جودة الفكر وقوة النظر . فالنقصان الحاصل بسبب اختلال البدن ، صار مجبوراً بما ذكرنا . السادس : إن كثرة الأفعال سبب الحصول الملكات الراسخة ، فصارت الزيادة الحاصلة بهذا الطريق ، جابرة للنقصان الحاصل بسبب اختلال البدن . السابع : قال عليه السلام : يهرم ابن آدم ، ويشيب منه اثنان : الحرص والأمل ، والتجارب دالة على صدق هذه القضية ، مع أن الحرص
The objection to it: We do not concede that if the rational faculty were bodily, it would necessarily become defective due to every defect in the body. Its explanation is from several aspects. First: Why may it not be said that the required amount of bodily health for the perfection of the rational faculty is a specific amount? And the perfection of the body's health is not considered in the perfection of the rational faculty. And if this is possible, it is not far off to say that this required amount remains until extreme old age, so the intellect remains perfect until extreme old age. Second: Perhaps the old man can continue in intellectual perceptions with soundness and perfection because his intellect is completed by an organ of the body that is postponed to corruption and change, even if affliction appears in the other powers and actions. Third: It is not impossible that some temperaments are more suitable for some powers, so perhaps the old man's temperament is more suitable for the rational faculty, and for this reason the rational powers become strong in him. Fourth: If the temperament is at the extreme of strength and intensity, then all other powers are strong, so the appetitive and irascible powers become very strong, and the strength of these powers prevents the intellect from perfection. So when old age occurs and weakness occurs, due to the weakness, weakness occurs in the intellect and weakness in these powers that prevent the intellect from perfection, and to the extent that weakness occurs in the intellect, weakness occurs in the opposites of the intellect, so the deficiency from one side is compensated by the deficiency occurring in the other side, and balance occurs. Fifth: The old man has preserved many premises, practiced them, and his experiences are many, and these states aid him in the quality of thought and strength of reflection. So the deficiency occurring due to the defect of the body becomes compensated by what we mentioned. Sixth: The multiplicity of actions is the cause of acquiring ingrained habits, so the increase gained through this path compensates for the deficiency occurring due to the defect of the body. Seventh: He [the Prophet] said, peace be upon him: 'The son of Adam ages and two things grow old in him: greed and hope.' And experiences indicate the truth of this proposition, even though greed
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents seven objections (i'tiradat) against the previous proof that the rational faculty is non-bodily. The opponent disputes the premise that if the rational faculty were bodily, then every bodily defect would cause a mental defect. The first objection suggests that only a specific minimum of bodily health is needed for rational perfection, and this minimum persists into old age, so the intellect remains intact. The second objection posits that the intellect may be linked to a specific bodily organ that deteriorates later than others, allowing continued rational activity. The third objection appeals to temperament: a particular temperament may be especially conducive to rational thought, and the old man's temperament happens to be such. The fourth objection offers a balancing argument: in youth, strong passions hinder intellectual development, but in old age, both the intellect and the passions weaken, resulting in a net equilibrium that allows the intellect to function. The fifth objection notes that the old man's accumulated knowledge, practice, and experience compensate for any physical decline. The sixth objection argues that repeated actions produce ingrained habits (malakat) that offset bodily deterioration. The seventh objection cites a prophetic hadith that greed and hope persist in old age, implying that certain mental qualities endure despite physical aging; this supports the idea that the rational faculty might be bodily yet remain strong. The seventh objection is incomplete due to page break.

البدن
المزاج

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These objections are meant to definitively refute the proof that the rational faculty is non-bodily.
Correction: The objections are presented as challenges to the proof; the author likely has responses later. The passage only lists the objections without endorsing them.

Checkpoint

A) First objection
B) Second objection
