First Objection to the Soul's Immateriality and Razi's Refutation
Razi presents the first objection that the self might be specific parts in the heart or brain, and answers it using the nature of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that the self might be specific parts in the heart or brain, and why this refutation proves the soul is a simple substance.

Core Idea
If you can know yourself while unaware of your body, your self is not a body.

Razi anticipates a natural objection: if the self is really the heart or a specific part of the brain, then being aware of oneself while not thinking about those organs poses no problem. He responds by invoking a deeper principle about extension (tahayyuz). This lesson walks through his answer and shows how it actually strengthens the proof for the soul's immateriality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's claim: the self might be specific parts in the heart or brain, and awareness of self could be awareness of those parts even when unaware of other organs.
Notice that the objection doesn't deny self-awareness; it offers a different explanation.
2
Examine Razi's premise on extension
Razi recalls an earlier proof: a body as an extended entity cannot be an attribute of something else; it is a self-subsistent essence.
This principle rules out the soul being an attribute of the body.
3
Follow the conditional argument
If the self were an extended substance, then knowing the self would necessarily mean knowing something extended. Otherwise one thing would be both known and unknown.
This is a logical necessity: knowledge of X requires knowledge of what X essentially is.
4
Consider the empirical evidence
During intense focus, a person knows his own self (saying 'I said this') while being unaware of extension and directions. This shows that one can know one's self without knowing anything extended.
This is a concrete observation that undermines the conditional's antecedent.
5
Conclude that the soul is a simple substance
Since the self knows itself without extension, it cannot be extended. Therefore it must be a non-extended simple substance (jawhar mujarrad).
This conclusion is the desired proof of the soul's immateriality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الأول: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: المعتبر في كون الإنسان إنساناً أجزاء مخصوصة في قلبه، أو في دماغه. فأما أن يكون المعتبر في كونه إنساناً هو قلبه مع شكله المخصوص، أو دماغه مع شكله المخصوص، فلا نقول به، وإذا كان كذلك فلا جرم أمكنه أن يعرف نفسه حال كونه غافلاً عن جميع الأعضاء كالقلب والدماغ وغيرهما؟ والجواب عن السؤال الأول: أن نقول: إنا قد دللنا في باب «نفي الهيولى»، أن كون الجسم جسماً متحيزاً، لا يمكن أن يكون صفة قائمة بمحل آخر، بل هو ذات قائمة بنفسها، مستقلة بذاتها. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: ذلك الإنسان إما أن يكون متحيزاً وإما أن لا يكون كذلك. فإن كانت ذاته جوهراً متحيزاً وجب أن يكون العالم بتلك الذات: عالماً بالمتحيز، وإلا لزم أن يكون الشيء الواحد معلوماً مجهولاً معاً. وهو محال. لكنه لا يجب ذلك، بدليل: أنه إذا كان شديد الاهتمام بأمر من الأمور، فإنه في تلك الساعة قد يقول: قلت كذا، وفعلت كذا. فهو في تلك الساعة عالم بذاته المخصوصة، مع أنه في تلك الساعة يكون غافلاً عن معنى التحيز الذاهب في الجهات الستة. فثبت: أنه لو كانت ذاته متحيزة، لكان كل من عرف ذاته، وجب أن يكون عارفاً بالمتحيز، وثبت: أنه قد يعرف ذاته، حال ما يكون غافلاً عن المتحيز، فوجب أن لا تكون ذاته متحيزة. وإذا لم تكن ذاته متحيزة، وجب أن تكون ذاته جوهراً مجرداً، وهو المطلوب.
The first question: Why is it not permissible to say: The consideration in a human being a human is specific parts in his heart or in his brain? So either the consideration in his being human is his heart with its specific form, or his brain with its specific form — we do not say that. And if so, then surely he can know himself while being unaware of all organs like the heart and brain and others? The answer to the first question: that we say: We have already proven in the chapter 'Negation of Hyle' that a body being a body, being extended, cannot be an attribute subsisting in another locus, but rather it is a self-subsistent essence, independent in itself. When this is established, we say: that human is either extended or not. If his essence is an extended substance, then it would be necessary that the knower of that essence be a knower of the extended; otherwise, one thing would be known and unknown simultaneously, which is impossible. But it is not necessary, as evidenced by the fact that when he is intensely preoccupied with some matter, at that moment he may say 'I said such-and-such, I did such-and-such.' So at that moment he is knowledgeable of his specific essence, even though he is unaware of the meaning of extension going in the six directions. So it is established: if his essence were extended, then whoever knows his essence would necessarily be aware of extension; and it is established that he may know his essence while being unaware of extension, so it is necessary that his essence not be extended. And if his essence is not extended, then his essence must be a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad). That is what is sought.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the preceding proof and its refutation. The objection asks: why not say that the essence of a human is specific parts in the heart or brain, so that the person is aware of his essence (those parts) even when unaware of other parts? The answer first invokes a previously established principle from the chapter on 'Negation of Hyle': that a body, being extended (mutahayyiz), cannot be an attribute inhering in something else; it is a self-subsistent essence. Then the argument proceeds: the human essence is either extended or not. If it were an extended substance (jawhar mutahayyiz), then knowledge of that essence would necessarily be knowledge of something extended; otherwise the same thing would be both known and unknown, which is impossible. But we observe that a person deeply focused can know his own essence (e.g., saying 'I said this') while being completely unaware of extension (al-tahayyuz) and directions. Therefore, his essence cannot be extended. Since it is not extended, it must be a simple substance (jawhar mujarrad), which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
متحيز (mutahayyiz, extended)
جوهر مجرد (jawhar mujarrad, simple substance)
هيولى (hayula, hyle)
صفة (sifa, attribute)
محل (mahall, locus)
ذات قائمة بنفسها (dhat qa'ima bi nafsiha, self-subsistent essence)
جهات ست (jihad sitt, six directions)
مطلوب (matlub, sought conclusion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection shows that the soul might be material parts in the heart or brain, so the soul's immateriality is not proven.
Correction: Razi's refutation turns the objection into a proof: because we know ourselves while unaware of any extended thing, the self cannot be extended at all, thus it is immaterial.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's answer to the first objection, what does knowledge of one's self prove about its nature?
A) It is an extended substance
B) It is a simple substance
C) It is the heart or brain
D) It is an accident

Key Takeaway
Razi's first objection is turned into a proof: self-awareness while unaware of extension proves the soul is a simple substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can know yourself right now, without thinking about your body. Does your daily life reflect that you believe your true self is other than your physical form? When worry or pain distracts you, do you forget that immaterial essence? How would your priorities shift if you truly internalised this proof?
?
Razi argues that when you are intensely focused, you are aware of your self but not of extension. Think of a moment when you were completely absorbed. Did you experience a sense of 'I' without any thought of your body? What does that tell you about what you really are? Will you take a moment today to observe yourself in that state, and thereby affirm the proof in your own consciousness?
?
If your essence is not your body, then your identity is not tied to your appearance, health, or age. Are you ready to stop defining yourself by your physical attributes? What would it cost you to let go of that identity? What would you gain? Commit today to one thought or action that affirms your self as a simple substance, not a collection of parts.

Third Proof: Soul Is Not a Body
The author classifies possibilities for the soul as body and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the third philosophical proof that the soul is not a body, including the disjunction and the refutation of the first alternative (soul as body qua body).

Core Idea
The soul cannot be a body because 'this human' is not a universal like 'body'.

The author now presents a powerful philosophical argument against the corporeality of the soul. He sets up a disjunction: if the soul were a body, it must be either body in the abstract or body with specific attributes. Both possibilities are impossible. This lesson walks through the disjunction and the proof against the first alternative. The argument for the second alternative is introduced but continues in the following passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without deep analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the disjunction and the author's conclusion stated upfront.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the second part of the proof is incomplete here.
2
Identify the disjunction and the two alternatives
The author says if the soul were a body, it would be either (1) body qua body (abstract body) or (2) body described by specific attributes. Mark these two possibilities.
3
Analyze the proof against the first alternative
The proof: 'body' is a concept common to all bodies, but 'this human' is not common. Therefore the soul (as principle of 'this human') cannot be identical to the abstract concept of body. Work through this reasoning.
This shows that the soul is more specific than mere body.
4
Reflect on the second alternative (incomplete)
The author begins the proof against the second alternative but the text is cut off. Anticipate that the full argument will show that a body with specific attributes is contingent and not suitable for the soul's immutability.

الحجة الثالثة : لو كانت النفس جسماً ، لكانت إما أن تكون عبارة عن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم ، أو عن الجسم الموصوف بالصفات المخصوصة . والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بكون النفس جسما . أما الحصر فظاهر ، وأما يمتنع كون النفس عبارة عن الجسم من حيث إنه جسم . فالدليل عليه هو : أن المفهوم من الجسم ، يصدق عليه كونه أمراً مشتركاً فيه بين جميع الأجسام . والمفهوم من كونه هذا الإنسان : لا يصدق عليه كونه أمراً مشتركاً فيه بين جميع الأجسام . وذلك يدل على أن المفهوم من كونه هذا الإنسان ليس مجرد كونه جسماً فقط، وأما أنه يمتنع كون النفس عبارة عن الجسم الموصوف بالصفات المخصوصة . فالدليل عليه : أن على هذا التقدير تكون ماهية النفس
The third proof: If the soul were a body, it would either be the body qua body, or the body described by specific attributes. Both divisions are false, so the statement that the soul is a body is invalid. As for the division, it is obvious. As for the impossibility of the soul being the body qua body, the proof for it is: the concept of body applies to something common among all bodies, whereas the concept of 'this human' does not apply to something common among all bodies. This indicates that the concept of 'this human' is not merely the concept of body. As for the impossibility of the soul being the body described by specific attributes, the proof for it: on this assumption, the quiddity of the soul would be...
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that the soul is not a body. The author begins by classifying the possibilities: if the soul were a body, it would either be the body taken simply as body (무반적인 جسم) or the body qualified with specific attributes (مثل البدن البشري). Both are impossible. The disjunction (حصار) is obvious. The proof for the first impossibility: the concept of 'body' is common to all bodies, while the concept of 'this human' is not; therefore, the soul (which is what makes a human 'this human') cannot be merely the concept of body. The text then begins the argument for the second impossibility but is cut off; the argument likely proceeds that if the soul were a body with specific attributes, it would be contingent and subject to change, contradicting the soul's immutability or simplicity. The conclusion that the soul is not a body is already stated at the beginning.

The Fifth Proof: Three Divisions of Possibility
Razi enumerates three divisions for body parts acquiring attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the fifth proof from Razi, which classifies three possible relations of dependence among body parts regarding the acquisition of attributes like life and knowledge.

Core Idea
The body's parts cannot all possess life and knowledge in any logical arrangement.

In this lesson, we encounter a compact but powerful argument from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. He presents the fifth proof against identifying the living, knowing being with the body itself. The proof works by enumerating every possible way the parts of a body could be receptive to attributes like life and power, then showing each possibility leads to absurdity. Even before the detailed refutation, the classification itself reveals the logical force of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the enumeration of three divisions. The translation will help you follow the logical structure.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the three divisions
The author lists three possibilities for how a body's parts might acquire attributes. Find each one and paraphrase it in your own words.
The divisions are based on dependence or independence among parts.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi states that all three divisions are false, so the original claim is false. Ask yourself: why would each division be problematic? The next lessons will fill in the reasons.
The proof is incomplete here; the refutation of each division is given later.
4
Reflect on the proof's strategy
This proof uses exhaustive division (taqsīm) to show that no possible scenario allows the body to be the living being. Notice how kalam relies on such logical partitioning.
This method is common in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : أن نقول : لو كان الحي القادر العالم هو الجسم ، لكان كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء قابلاً لهذه الصفات ، إما أن يتوقف على كون الآخر كذلك ، وإما أن لا يتوقف حصول الاستعداد في أحد الجانبين على الثاني ، ولا يتوقف الثاني على الأول . وإما أن يستغني كل واحد من الجانبين عن الثاني . والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة ، فكان القول بأن الحي القادر العالم : هو الجسم باطلاً
The fifth proof: that we say: if the living, powerful, knowing being is the body, then each one of those parts would be receptive to these attributes, either depending on the other being so, or not depending—the acquisition of preparedness in one side does not depend on the second, nor does the second depend on the first. Or each side dispenses with the other. And the three divisions are false, so the statement that the living, powerful, knowing being is the body is false.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-ḥujjah al-khāmisah) against the claim that the living, powerful, knowing being is the body. The author divides the possible ways a body's parts could be receptive to attributes like life, knowledge, and power into three exhaustive divisions: (1) each part's acquisition of these attributes depends on another part also acquiring them; (2) there is no dependence at all—each part's preparedness is independent of the others; (3) one part depends on another while the other is independent. The author then declares all three divisions false without immediate explanation, concluding that the original claim is false. This sets up the detailed refutation that follows in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الحجة الخامسة
الأقسام الثلاثة
الاستعداد
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof is complete and self-contained in this passage, but the reasoning for why each division is false is not given here.
Correction: The passage only states the divisions and asserts their falsity; the detailed refutation is provided in the subsequent lesson.

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof uses exhaustive division to show that no logical arrangement of body parts acquiring attributes is possible, thus the living being cannot be the body.

Refutation of the First Two Divisions and Start of the Third
Razi refutes the first two divisions and begins refuting the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical arguments Razi uses to refute the first two divisions of parts (mutual dependence and independence) and the beginning of his argument against the third division (one-sided dependence).

Core Idea
Razi refutes three theories of parts using circularity, absurdity of multiplicity, and the principle of similarity.

In this passage, Razi systematically dismantles three possible ways the parts of a human could relate in acquiring attributes. He first shows that mutual dependence leads to a vicious circle, then demonstrates that complete independence produces contradictory states that violate necessary knowledge. Finally, he begins to argue against one-sided dependence by appealing to the similarity of parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very structured argumentative style with clear divisions.
2
Understand the refutation of the first division
Identify the first division (mutual dependence) and see how Razi argues it leads to a vicious circle (dawr), which is logically invalid.
The key term here is الدور (vicious circle).
3
Understand the refutation of the second division
The second division (complete independence) is refuted by showing two types of impossibilities: (1) all parts become separate individuals, making one human many; (2) contradictory states can coexist among parts, like one knowing and another ignorant.
Razi appeals to necessary knowledge (العلم الضروري) to assert these are impossible.
4
Examine the beginning of the third division's refutation
The third division (one-sided dependence) is introduced and Razi gives the first reason: the parts are similar in essence, so they cannot differ in their necessary attributes and consequences.
Note that this argument is incomplete; the second reason is missing due to a page break.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Razi systematically eliminates all three possibilities, leaving only the correct view that parts depend on the soul for attributes.
This is part of a larger discussion on the unity of the self.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما بيان أن القسم الأول : باطل : فهو أنه لما كان حصول الاستعداد في هذا الجزء يتوقف على حصول الاستعداد في ذلك الجزء ، وهذا التوقف حاصل من الجانب الثاني ، فحينئذ يلزم توقف كل واحد منهما على الآخر ، وذلك دور ، والدور باطل . وأما بيان أن القسم الثاني باطل : فلان على هذا التقدير يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء مستقلا لقبول الحياة والموت والجهل والعلم والقدرة والعجز ، وذلك يوجب القول بحصول نوعين من أنواع المحالات : الأول : إن بتقدير أن تحصل الحياة والعلم والقدرة في جميع الأجزاء ، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد منها حياً عالماً قادراً على حدة ، وحينئذ لا يكون الإنسان الواحد إنساناً واحداً، بل إناساً كثيرين. والعلم بفساده ضروري . والنوع الثاني من المحالات : إنه إذا كان حصول هذه الحالة لكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء لا يتوقف على حصولها للجزء الثاني ، فحينئذ لا يمتنع أحد تلك الأجزاء ميتا جاهلاً عاجزاً حال كون الثاني حياً عالماً قادراً ، ولا يمتنع أيضاً : اتصاف أحد الجزئين بالعلم بالشيء حال اتصاف الجزء الثاني بالجهل بذلك الشيء . ولا يمتنع أن يحاول أحد الجزئين فعلا ، ويحاول الجزء الثاني دفعه ومنعه . وحينئذ يصير حال تلك الأجزاء ، مساوياً لحال جماعة من الناس ، [؟] عالماً كون بعضهم بالشيء حال ما يكون البعض مانعاً منه ، ولما كان العلم الضروري حاصلا بامتناع هذه المعاني ، ثبت فساد هذا القسم . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو توقف أحد الجانبين على الآخر ، واستغناء الآخر عن الأول فهذا أيضاً باطل لوجهين : الأول : إن تلك الأجزاء متشابهة متماثلة ، بناء على القول بتماثل الأجسام . والأشياء المتماثلة في الماهية يمتنع اختلافها في اللوازم والنتائج
As for the explanation that the first division is false: it is that when the acquisition of preparedness in this part depends on the acquisition of preparedness in that part, and this dependence occurs from the second side, then it follows that each depends on the other, which is a vicious circle, and a vicious circle is false. As for the explanation that the second division is false: because on this assumption, each one of those parts would be independent in accepting life, death, ignorance, knowledge, power, and incapacity, and that necessitates the occurrence of two types of impossibilities: the first: that assuming that life, knowledge, and power occur in all parts, then each one of them would be living, knowing, powerful individually, and then the one human would not be one human but many humans. The knowledge of its corruption is necessary. The second type of impossibilities: that if the occurrence of this state for each one of those parts does not depend on its occurrence for the second part, then it is not impossible that one of those parts is dead, ignorant, incapable while the second is living, knowing, powerful; nor is it impossible that one part is described with knowledge of a thing while the second part is described with ignorance of that thing; nor is it impossible that one part attempts an action while the second part attempts to repel and prevent it. Then the state of those parts becomes similar to the state of a group of people, [؟] some of them knowing a thing while some are preventing it. And since necessary knowledge exists of the impossibility of these meanings, the corruption of this division is established. As for the third division: which is the dependence of one side on the other and the dispensation of the other from the first, this is also false for two reasons: the first: that those parts are similar and equivalent, based on the assertion of the similarity of bodies. And things similar in essence cannot differ in their necessary concomitants and consequences.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed refutation of the three divisions stated in the previous lesson. The author begins with the first division (mutual dependence). He argues that if each part's preparedness depends on the other, then a vicious circle (dawr) results, which is logically impossible. For the second division (complete independence), he argues that if each part independently acquires attributes, two absurdities follow: First, if all parts simultaneously have life, knowledge, and power, each part becomes a separate living, knowing, powerful entity, making the single human actually multiple humans—contradicting necessary knowledge. Second, independence allows contradictory states: one part could be dead while another is alive, one part knowing what another is ignorant of, or one part acting while another opposes—similar to a group of separate people. Since necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) affirms these impossibilities, the second division is false. The author then begins refuting the third division (one-sided dependence). He gives a first reason: the parts are similar in essence (based on the principle of body similarity), and things identical in essence cannot differ in their necessary attributes and consequences. The argument is cut off, but the conclusion is that such one-sided dependence is also impossible, completing the refutation of all three divisions.

Key Terms
الاستعداد
الدور
المحالات
العلم الضروري
تماثل الأجسام
اللوازم والنتائج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the third division is fully refuted in this passage.
Correction: Only the first reason for the third division's falsehood is given; the second reason is missing due to a page break, so the refutation is incomplete in this passage.

Checkpoint

B) Infinite regress
C) False dilemma
D) Argument from ignorance

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the first two divisions of parts by showing they lead to circularity and absurd consequences, and begins his refutation of the third by appealing to the similarity of parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt as if you were multiple persons inside one body—one part wanting good, another part wanting evil? How does Razi's argument that the parts cannot be independent challenge the way you view your own inner conflicts? Will you continue to excuse your sins by blaming a 'part' of you?

Razi says that if parts were independent, each would be a separate self. But if you are a single self, then your entire being is accountable for every thought and deed. Does this make you more responsible for your actions? Will you now take full ownership of your choices, knowing that no 'part' of you is exempt?
?
The principle of similarity states that things identical in essence cannot differ in their consequences. Since your limbs and organs are similar in being 'parts' of you, how can you claim that some actions are 'just from your hand' while others are 'from your heart'? Are you ready to see that every part of you testifies to the same reality—whether you submit to Allah or rebel?

The Perceiver of All Perceptions Must Be One
The author concludes a proof that the perceiver of all perceptions must be one entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the perceiver of all universal and particular perceptions must be a single entity, as demonstrated by the three rational operations of analysis, affirmation, and negation.

Core Idea
One mind must handle all perceptions and their relations.

This passage presents the concluding proof that the one who perceives both universals and particulars and performs operations like analysis and composition must be a single entity. The author shows that the same entity that ascribes universals to particulars in three ways—analysis, affirmation, and negation—cannot be multiple. This conclusion is presented as irrefutable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author mentions three operations before stating the conclusion.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the three operations
The passage lists three ways the perceiver relates universals to particulars: analysis (الإنحلال), affirmation (الإيجاب), and negation (السلب). Reflect on how these cover all logical relations.
These operations are fundamental to rational thought.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the one described with all perceptions and who handles them by analysis and composition must be one thing. Ask yourself: why can't different entities handle different operations?
The unity of the operations points to the unity of the agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلي، حتى يمكنه أن ينسب ذلك الكلي إلى ذلك الجزئي تارة بالإنحلال وتارة بالإيجاب، وتارة بالسلب. فظهر بهذا البرهان الذي لا ريب فيه: أن الموصوف بجميع الإدراكات الكلية والجزئية، وأن المتصرف فيها بالتحليل والتركيب، يجب أن يكون شيئاً واحداً. وهو المطلوب
The universal, so that he can ascribe that universal to that particular sometimes by analysis, sometimes by affirmation, and sometimes by negation. So it appears by this proof in which there is no doubt: that the one described with all universal and particular perceptions, and the one who handles them by analysis and composition, must be one thing. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the concluding proof of a previous argument. The author demonstrates that the perceiver (al-mudrik) of all perceptions must be a single entity. He argues that a person relates universals to particulars in three ways: by analysis (al-inhilal), affirmation (al-ijab), or negation (al-salb). The one who performs these operations must be the same entity that perceives both universals and particulars. Therefore, the perceiver and the one who analyzes and combines must be one thing. This is the desired conclusion (al-matlub).

The Unity of Perception, Desire, and Action
The author proves the identity of perceiver, desirer, and agent, and refutes Galen's separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the perceiving entity must be the same as the entity that desires, hates, and acts, and why Galen's separation of heart and brain is incoherent.

Core Idea
The same entity must perceive, desire, and act; separating them leads to contradiction.

In this passage, the author presents a compelling argument that the perceiver and the doer are one and the same. He builds it on the mechanics of voluntary action: belief leads to desire, which leads to action. Then he refutes Galen's claim that emotions reside in the heart while perception is in the brain, showing that emotions require cognition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from belief to desire to action.
The argument is built on a chain: belief → desire/aversion → action/refraining.
2
Identify the key link between perception and desire
The author argues that desire and aversion arise from belief (perception). Thus the entity that desires must also be the one that perceives, otherwise the desire would occur without cause.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Trace the identity chain
The agent is the one who wills, and the willer is the perceiver, so agent equals perceiver. Follow this chain carefully.
The author states it as a decisive proof.
4
Examine the refutation of Galen
Galen separates emotion and perception. The author points out that emotions like anger require a belief (perception), so their locus must also perceive.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في بيان أن ذلك الشيء الواحد، الذي هو المدرك، يجب أن يكون بعينه هو الفاصل لجميع الأفعال: والدليل عليه: أن الإنسان إنما يفعل بالقصد والاختيار. والفعل الاختياري معناه: أنه إذا اعتقد في الشيء، أنه مشتمل على نفع خالص، أو راجح. أوجب هذا الاعتقاد حصول رغبة في ذلك الفعل، وإذا حصلت تلك الرغبة، صار القادر فاعلاً. وأما إذا اعتقد أن في الفعل الفلاني مفسدة خالصة أو راجحة، أوجب ذلك الاعتقاد حصول نفرة عن ذلك الفعل وإذا حصلت تلك النفرة، صار القادر ممتنعاً عن الفعل. وإذا ثبت هذا، ثبت أن ذلك المدرك بعينه، يجب أن يكون هو الموصوف بالرغبة تارة، وبالنفرة أخرى، إذ لو كان الموصوف بالرغبة والنفرة شيء مغاير لذلك المدرك لكانت هذه الرغبة والنفرة، حاصلة لشيء خالي عن الإدراك والاعتقاد. وحينئذ يكون حصول تلك الرغبة، وتلك النفرة، لا لأجل ذلك الاعتقاد. وقد فرضناه معللاً به. هذا خلف. وأيضاً: فالراغب في الشيء أو النافر عنه، قد أشار فهمه إلى ذلك الشيء المرغوب فيه أو المهروب منه فثبت: أن المريد للفعل أو الترك، يجب أن يكون هو بعينه مدركاً لذلك الشيء. وظاهر أن الفاعل للفعل هو الذي أراد ذلك الفعل، ولما ثبت أن الفاعل هو بعينه ذلك الشيء، الذي أراده، وثبت: أن ذلك المريد هو بعينه الذي أدركه، ثبت أن الفاعل هو بعينه ذلك المدرك. وهذا برهان قاطع على أن الفاعل والمزيد والمدرك: شيء واحد بعينه. وأن توزيع هذه الأحوال على هذه الذوات المتباينة: قول لا يليق بالعاقل البتة. ومن العجائب جداً في هذا الباب: أن جالينوس، قال: القلب مبدأ الغضب والفرح والغم، ثم قال: وليس في القلب قوة مدركة، وإنما القوة المدركة حاصلة في الدماغ. وهذا القول لا يليق بالعاقل لأنه ما لم يحصل اعتقاد كون ذلك الشيء منافياً للمزاج، امتنع صيرورته مغضوباً عليه، وما لم يحصل اعتقاد أنه ملائم للمزاج امتنع حصول الفرح به. فالقول بأن القلب محل الغم والفرح والغضب. مع كونه خالياً عن الإدراك: قول لا يليق بالعاقل البتة. فثبت بما ذكرنا من البيان الظاهر الجلي القوي: أن المدرك لجميع أنواع الإدراكات، وأن المريد والكاره، وأن الفاعل والتارك، يجب أن يكون شيئاً واحداً.
The second introduction: explaining that that one thing, which is the perceiver, must be the very same one that distinguishes all actions. The proof: man acts by intention and choice. The meaning of voluntary action: when he believes that something contains pure or preponderant benefit, this belief necessitates the occurrence of desire for that action, and when that desire occurs, the capable person becomes an agent. But if he believes that a certain action contains pure or preponderant harm, that belief necessitates the occurrence of aversion from that action, and when that aversion occurs, the capable person refrains from action. If this is established, then it is established that that very perceiver must be the one described sometimes with desire and sometimes with aversion, for if the one described with desire and aversion were something other than that perceiver, then this desire and aversion would occur to something devoid of perception and belief. Then the occurrence of that desire and aversion would not be due to that belief, whereas we assumed it to be caused by it. This is a contradiction. Also, the one who desires or avoids something directs his understanding to that desired or fled-from thing, so it is established that the one who wills action or refraining must be the very same one who perceives that thing. And it is apparent that the agent of the action is the one who willed that action, and since it is established that the agent is the very same thing that willed it, and it is established that that willer is the very same one who perceived it, it is established that the agent is the very same perceiver. This is a decisive proof that the agent, the desirer, and the perceiver are one identical thing, and that distributing these states among different entities is a statement unworthy of an intelligent person. Among the astonishing things in this chapter: Galen said: the heart is the seat of anger, joy, and grief. Then he said: there is no perceiving power in the heart; rather, the perceiving power is located in the brain. This statement is unworthy of an intelligent person, because if there is no belief that something is contrary to the temperament, it cannot become an object of anger, and if there is no belief that it is compatible with the temperament, joy cannot occur. So the statement that the heart is the locus of grief, joy, and anger while being devoid of perception is a statement utterly unworthy of an intelligent person. Thus it is established by the clear, evident, and strong exposition we have mentioned: that the perceiver of all types of perceptions, the desirer and hater, and the agent and refraining one, must be one thing.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the second introduction, aiming to prove that the same entity that perceives must also be the one that acts. The author explains voluntary action: it occurs when a belief about benefit or harm leads to desire or aversion. The one who desires or avers must be the same as the one who perceives, otherwise desire would arise without cognition, which is contradictory. Furthermore, the agent is the one who wills, and the willer is the one who perceives, so agent and perceiver are identical. The author then refutes Galen's view that the heart experiences emotions while the brain perceives. The author argues that emotions like anger and joy depend on belief, so the seat of emotions must also perceive. Thus the perceiver and the agent/desirer are one.

المريد
الكاره
الفاعل
المدرك
جالينوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Emotions and perceptions can be located in different parts of the body (e.g., heart for emotions, brain for perception).
Correction: The author argues that emotions like anger and joy depend on beliefs (perceptions), so the seat of emotions must also have perception; thus they must be in the same entity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is Galen's claim that the heart is the seat of emotions but not perception untenable?
A) The heart cannot feel
B) Emotions like anger require belief, which is a perception
C) The brain is too small
D) Galen was not a Muslim

Key Takeaway
The perceiver, desirer, and agent are one identical entity; separating them leads to logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you often act on desires without pausing to examine the belief behind them? How does this lesson challenge you to bring your perceptions and actions into alignment? Commit today to scrutinize one desire before acting on it.
?
Think of a recent emotion—anger, joy, or grief. Can you trace it back to a specific belief or judgment you made? If your heart and mind were separate, as Galen claimed, what would that imply about the truthfulness of your feelings? Are you willing to take full ownership of your emotional responses as flowing from your own perceptions?
?
The author insists that distributing perception, desire, and action among different entities is 'unworthy of an intelligent person.' How does this affect your view of your own self? Do you live as a fractured being, or as a unified whole accountable to your Creator? What change must you make to live in the integrity of being one self before Allah?

The Soul is Not a Body: Initial Proof
Opening of a proof that the soul is not a body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument that the soul cannot be a body because it is one entity, and the enumeration of possibilities that forms the first proof.

Core Idea
The soul's oneness proves it is neither a body nor lodged in one.

The author opens the third introduction by declaring that the soul, being a single entity, cannot be a body or an accident. He then launches a proof by enumerating the possibilities for what a body could be, arguing that most are invalid. This lesson examines that opening argument, noting that the passage is incomplete and contains a minor inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the passage cuts off mid-sentence at the end of the page.
The passage is a fragment; the full argument is not present.
2
Identify the enumerated possibilities
The author lists four possibilities: (1) the body is the entire body, (2) a body inside it, (3) inside an organ, or (4) outside it. However, he then says 'the three divisions are invalid.' This is an inconsistency—there are four items, not three.
This inconsistency may be a scribal error; check the original source if possible.
3
Reflect on the argument's logic
Why does the author start by saying the soul is 'one thing'? Because if it were a body, it would be composite, contradicting its oneness. The enumeration helps him systematically eliminate possibilities.
The argument for why it cannot be the whole body is just beginning—he mentions faculties like hearing and sight are not in the whole body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة في بيان أنه لما كان العالم القادر شيئاً واحداً، وجب أن يكون ذلك الشيء غير جسم وغير حال في الجسم:
والدليل عليه وجهان:
الأول: إنه لو كان جسماً، لكان ذلك الجسم. إما أن يكون مجموع هذا البدن، أو جسماً داخلاً فيه، أو داخلاً في عضو من الأعضاء الموجودة فيه، أو جسماً خارجاً عنه. والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة
وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن يكون ذلك الجسم عبارة عن مجموع ذلك البدن، لأنا نعلم بأن السمع والبصر والتخيل والتفكر والتذكر، غير حاصل في
The third introduction: explaining that since the knowing, powerful one is one thing, it is necessary that that thing be not a body nor inherent in a body. The proof has two aspects: First: if it were a body, then that body would either be the entirety of this body, or a body inside it, or inside one of the organs present in it, or a body outside it. The three divisions are invalid. And we say: it is impossible for that body to be the entire body, because we know that hearing, sight, imagination, reflection, and recollection are not present in
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the third introduction, which argues that the soul (or the active intellect) is neither a body nor an accident in a body. The author gives two proofs; only the first is partially presented here. The first proof enumerates the possibilities for a body: it could be the whole body, a part within it, a part of an organ, or outside it. The author claims that three of these are invalid (though the text says 'three divisions' despite listing four). He begins to explain why it cannot be the whole body: because faculties like hearing, sight, imagination, reflection, and recollection are not present in it. The argument is cut off.

Correction: The author argues that the soul is one and cannot be a body or part of a body, so it is not identical to the body nor localized in a bodily organ.

?

What does this tell you about where your true identity resides? Will you act on that knowledge before you leave this body?

Refutation of the Soul as an External Body
Al-Razi refutes the third division and concludes the soul is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi argues that the soul cannot be a body external to the body, and how this leads to the conclusion that the soul is not a body at all.

Core Idea
Al-Razi shows that the soul is not a body by eliminating all possibilities.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi continues his refutation of those who claim the soul is a body. Having already considered two possibilities, he now examines the third: that the soul is a body external to the physical body. He declares this false by necessity and concludes that the soul is entirely non-corporeal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow from stating the third division to concluding its falsehood.
The Arabic uses the phrase 'معلوم الفساد بالضرورة', which is key.
2
Understand 'known to be false by necessity'
Reflect on what it means for something to be 'known to be false by necessity'. It implies immediate rational insight without need for further proof.
This is a strong claim, indicating self-evident falsehood.
3
Trace the logical inference
Identify how al-Razi moves from the falsehood of the third division to the general conclusion that the soul is not a body. Consider the role of the other two divisions (not mentioned here) in this inference.
The conclusion rests on the invalidation of all three divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يكون ذلك الشيء جسماً خارجاً عن البدن ، فهو معلوم الفساد بالضرورة. وإذا بطلت هذه الأقسام الثلاثة ، ثبت : أن ذلك الشيء ليس بجسم.
As for the third division: that that thing is a body external to the body, it is known to be false by necessity. And when these three divisions are invalidated, it is established: that that thing is not a body.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage concludes a preceding argument by examining the third possible way the soul could be a body: that it is a body external to the physical body. Al-Razi asserts that this possibility is known to be false by immediate necessity (ḍarūra). Having invalidated all three divisions (presumably including the soul being the body itself or a part of it), he concludes that the soul is not a body at all. This is a concise deductive step: if all possible ways for X to be a body are false, then X is not a body.

Key Terms
قسم
جسم
فساد بالضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul being a 'body external to the body' means it is a separate physical entity like a spirit, and that this is plausible.
Correction: Al-Razi denies that the soul is a body at all; the external body possibility is self-evidently false.

Checkpoint

A) The soul is a subtle body.
B) The soul is not a body.
C) The soul is part of the body.
D) The soul is a body external to the body.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proves the soul is not a body by showing that every possible way it could be a body is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the soul is not a body. Yet you live every day as if your body is all that matters. How will this truth change your daily choices? Commit to one action today that treats your soul as the real you.
?
Al-Razi proves the soul is not a body by necessity. If you truly believed your soul is immaterial and eternal, what would you stop fearing? What would you start pursuing?
?
The argument requires you to accept something as self-evident. But are you living as if the soul's immateriality is obvious? If not, what is holding you back from acting on this certainty?

Second Argument: The Soul is Not Spatially Extended
Al-Razi argues that the soul is not a spatially extended thing (mutahayyiz) by exhausting divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's second argument against the soul being spatially extended, including the exhaustive dichotomy of divisibility and the refutation of both alternatives.

Core Idea
The soul is not spatially extended because both divisibility and indivisibility are impossible for it.

Al-Razi continues his series of proofs by presenting a second argument that the soul is not a spatially extended thing (mutahayyiz). He sets up an exhaustive dichotomy: if the soul were extended, it would be either divisible or not divisible. He then systematically shows both possibilities lead to contradictions, referencing arguments from previous proofs. This lesson walks through that logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dichotomy
Recognize that al-Razi begins by dividing the possibility of a spatially extended soul into two exhaustive options: divisible or not divisible.
This is a classic logical technique: case analysis.
2
Refutation of Divisibility
Learn why a divisible extended soul cannot possess the attributes of the soul (like knowledge) – because it would require the whole or a part to have those attributes, both problematic.
Al-Razi references a previous proof here (الدليل المتقدم).
3
Refutation of Indivisibility
Understand that if the soul is indivisible (an atom), either one rejects atoms (then soul can't be extended) or accepts atoms but then the same reasoning from the first proof applies.
4
Draw the Conclusion
Realize that by eliminating both possibilities, al-Razi concludes the soul is not spatially extended.
This completes the second proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني في بيان أن ذلك الشيء ليس بمتحيز : هو أن نقول : ذلك المتحيز ، إما أن يكون منقسماً ، أو لا يكون منقسماً . والقسمان باطلان . أما أن يكون منقسماً . فلأن الموصوف بهذه الصفات ، إما أن يكون مجموع تلك الأقسام ، أو كل واحد منها أو واحد منها معيناً . والكل باطل ، لعين أنه يمتنع البيان المذكور في الدليل المتقدم . وأما أنه يمتنع أن يكون غير منقسم ، فهو إنا إن نفينا الجوهر الفرد ، فقد تم المقصود ، وإن أثبتناه أبطلناه بعين ما ذكرناه في الدليل الأول والله أعلم.
And the second aspect in explaining that that thing is not spatially extended is that we say: that spatially extended thing is either divisible or not divisible. Both divisions are false. As for it being divisible: because the thing described by these attributes is either the sum of those parts, or each one of them, or one specific part of them. All are false, due to the same reason it is impossible as mentioned in the preceding proof. And as for it being impossible that it is not divisible: then if we negate the indivisible atom, the purpose is achieved; and if we affirm it, we invalidate it by the very same [argument] we mentioned in the first proof. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument that the soul is not spatially extended (mutahayyiz). Al-Razi sets up a dichotomy: if the soul were extended, it would be either divisible or not divisible. He argues both alternatives are impossible. If divisible, the soul would be composed of parts, but the thing possessing the attributes of the soul (knowledge, etc.) cannot be a composite because that leads to problems (referencing an earlier proof). If not divisible (i.e., an indivisible atom), then either one denies the existence of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard), in which case the goal is reached, or one affirms it, but then the same reasoning from the first proof refutes it. Thus the soul is not extended.

Key Terms
متحيز
منقسم
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'not divisible' simply means the soul is an indivisible atom, and that this is a viable option.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that even if one affirms the indivisible atom, the same reasoning from the first proof invalidates it; thus the 'not divisible' branch also fails.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, if the soul is spatially extended, what are the two possibilities?
A) It is either a body or a spirit.
B) It is either divisible or not divisible.
C) It is either inside the body or outside.
D) It is either eternal or created.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's second argument eliminates both divisible and indivisible conceptions of a spatially extended soul, proving it cannot be extended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is not extended in space, then your true self is not located anywhere in the physical world. How does that change the way you think about death, which is the separation of soul and body? Are you living with the certainty that your soul—your real 'you'—cannot be harmed by any physical threat?
?
Al-Razi's argument shows that the soul cannot be divided. Yet your attention, loyalty, and love are often divided between many things. What does it mean to live as if your soul is one unified reality, and how would you realign your life to reflect that oneness?
?
If the soul is not spatially extended, then God's closeness to you is not a matter of physical proximity. Does your prayer and remembrance of God reflect a nearness that transcends space? If not, what would have to change in your daily practice to truly feel that intimacy?

The Soul is One: A Necessary Knowledge
Al-Razi proves the soul is one by necessary self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that the soul is known to be one thing through necessary self-knowledge, and how this refutes the idea that the self is a composite of multiple parts.

Core Idea
The soul's oneness is known by direct awareness, not by inference.

Al-Razi begins by asserting that the most evident knowledge is our awareness of our own existence and oneness. He then provides a proof: if the self were multiple parts, each part would know itself but not the others, so the aggregate would not have comprehensive self-knowledge. Yet we know ourselves directly as one. This lesson examines this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice the two sub-arguments within the first point.
2
Identify the two sub-arguments
The passage first argues for knowledge of existence, then for knowledge of oneness. Understand each.
The first sub-argument is about self-awareness preceding all knowledge.
3
Understand the reductio argument
Focus on the conditional: if the self were many, then no comprehensive knowledge; but we have comprehensive knowledge, so the self is one.
This is a crucial logical move.

الحجة السابعة : إن نفس كل أحد شيء واحد . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، امتنع كونه جسماً . أما بيان المقام الأول : فهو أن أعرف المعارف، وأظهر الظاهرات : علم كل أحد بأنه موجود ، وعلمه بأنه واحد [بكوني؟] . أما بيان الأول : فالدليل عليه أني إذا علمت شيئاً فإني أحكم على نفسي [عالماً لك المعلوم؟] ، والحكم بشيء على شيء ، مسبوق بتصور الطرفين، وهذا يقتضي أن علمي بذاتي، كان سابقاً على علمي بذلك الشيء ، حتى حكمت على نفسي بكونها عالمة بذلك المعلوم . وأما بيان الثاني : فالدليل عليه : أني لو لم أكن شيئاً واحداً ، بل كنت أشياء كثيرة ، وأنا عبارة عن مجموعها . لكان كل واحد من تلك الأشياء عالماً بحال نفسه ، ويكون غافلا عن حال الآخر . وحينئذ لا يكون ذلك المجموع عالماً بمجموع تلك الأحوال . فلو كنت أشياء كثيرة ، لوجب أن لا أكون عالماً بأحوال نفسي ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل ، بل نقول : إن من المعلوم بالضرورة أن الذي أشير إليه بقولي ( أنا ) : شيء واحد [منفرد] ، وغير متعدد . وأن الفرق بين علمي بأني أنا شيء واحد وبين الحكم على مجموع أشخاص من الناس بأنهم مجموع واحد : معلوم بالضرورة ، بل هو من أظهر العلوم الضرورية . فثبت : أن نفس كل [أحد] شيء واحد . وأما المتحيز ، والحال في المتحيز ، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون واحداً ، بناء على نفي الجوهر الفرد ، وأما على القول بإثبات [الجوهر الفرد] فإنه يمتنع أيضاً أن تكون النفس جزءاً لا يتجزأ . وذلك يدل على أن النفس ليست بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز . وهو المطلوب معاً . فهذا شرح جملة الوجوه القوية في إثبات تجرد النفس.
The seventh proof: The soul of everyone is one thing. And when this is the case, it is impossible for it to be a body. As for explaining the first point: it is that the most known of known things and the most apparent of apparent things is the knowledge of everyone that he exists, and his knowledge that he is one [with my being?]. As for explaining the first [knowledge of existence]: the proof for it is that when I know a thing I judge myself to be [knowing that thing?], and judging something about something is preceded by conceptualization of both extremes, and this requires that my knowledge of my essence was prior to my knowledge of that thing, until I judged myself to be knowing that known. As for explaining the second [knowledge of oneness]: the proof for it is that if I were not one thing but many things, and I am the sum of them, then each one of those things would know its own state and be ignorant of the state of the other, and then that sum would not know the totality of those states. So if I were many things, it would be necessary that I not know my own states, and it is known that this is false. Rather we say: it is known by necessity that what I refer to by my saying 'I' is one single thing, not multiple. And the difference between my knowledge that I am one thing and the judgment upon a group of people that they are one group is known by necessity; indeed it is among the most evident of necessary knowledge. So it is established: that the soul of everyone is one thing. As for the spatially extended thing and what is in the spatially extended thing, it is impossible for it to be one, based on the negation of the indivisible atom; and on the view affirming the indivisible atom, it is also impossible for the soul to be an indivisible part. And that indicates that the soul is not spatially extended nor a state in something spatially extended. That is the desired conclusion together. This is an explanation of the set of strong arguments in proving the immateriality of the soul.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This is the seventh proof, consisting of two main parts: first establishing that the soul is one, then showing that a body cannot be one. Al-Razi begins by stating the premise: every soul is one thing; if so, it cannot be a body. To prove the first part, he appeals to self-evident knowledge: everyone knows they exist and that they are one. The knowledge of one's existence is foundational, as when we know anything we first know our own existence. The knowledge of oneness is proven by imagining the opposite: if the self were multiple parts, each part would know itself but not the others, so the aggregate would not have comprehensive knowledge; but we know ourselves directly as one, not as a collection. This is a necessary knowledge, distinct from judging a group as one. Thus the soul is one. Then he argues that a spatially extended entity cannot be truly one: if one denies atoms, extension implies composition; if one affirms atoms, the soul cannot be an atom. Therefore the soul is neither extended nor in an extended thing, establishing its immateriality. The concluding sentence indicates that these are among the strong proofs for the soul's immateriality.

Key Terms
نفس
واحد
علم ضروري
أنا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the soul's unity is metaphorical or composite, like a unified group.
Correction: Al-Razi insists that the soul's oneness is real and known by necessity, distinct from the conventional unity of a group.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi prove that the soul is one thing?
A) By citing scripture.
B) By showing that if it were multiple, one would not know one's own states.
C) By analyzing the body's composition.
D) By arguing from the essence of God.

Key Takeaway
The soul's oneness is directly known by necessity, not a product of inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know that you exist. But do you live as if your existence is owed to the One who says 'He is One'? What would change if you truly felt the directness of your own being as a sign of His oneness?
?
Al-Razi says that if you were composite, you could not know your own states. When you feel torn between duty and desire, are you experiencing the composite self he refutes, or a test of your unified soul? How will you respond to that pull today?
?
If your awareness of your own 'I' is a necessary knowledge, then your accountability before Allah is also necessary. How does this immediate self-knowledge call you to an immediate turn (tawbah) without delay?

Why the Soul Cannot be a Body
Al-Razi argues that no spatially extended entity can be one, so the soul is immaterial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that a spatially extended entity cannot be truly one, and therefore the soul cannot be a body or in a body.

Core Idea
No body can be truly one—only the soul is.

Having established that the soul is one, Al-Razi now argues that a body cannot be one. He considers two positions on atomism and shows that on both, a spatially extended thing is not a genuine unity. Therefore, the soul is neither a body nor located in a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two scenarios.
The passage is concise but packed.
2
Identify the two scenarios
First, if one denies the indivisible atom, extended things are composite and thus not one. Second, if one affirms the indivisible atom, the soul cannot be that atom because an atom is still a part.
Al-Razi covers both possibilities.
3
Understand the conclusion
The soul is not extended (body) nor in an extended thing (like a property); it is immaterial.
This completes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How Contradictory Effects Prove the Soul's Immateriality
Al-Razi proves the soul is distinct from the body using the principle that one thing cannot cause both perfection and deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the rational principle that one thing cannot cause both perfection and deficiency to prove that the soul is distinct from the body.

Core Idea
One cause cannot produce opposite effects; the soul's perfection and body's deficiency from the same activity show they are distinct.

Al-Razi presents a sophisticated argument that relies on a fundamental rational principle: a single cause cannot be the source of both perfection and deficiency for the same thing. By observing that intense intellectual activity perfects the soul while harming the body, he demonstrates that the soul cannot be identical to the body or any part of it. This proof is a classic example of using observable effects to establish a metaphysical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rational principle
Read the first sentence: 'The intuition of reason judges that one thing with respect to one thing cannot be a cause of its perfection and a cause of its deficiency.' This is the foundational axiom of the proof.
This is a self-evident principle in rational thought.
2
Observe the effects of intellectual activity
Notice that persistent thinking in precise sciences brings perfection to the soul (making it luminous, joyful) but harms the body (dries the brain, weakens digestion). The same cause produces opposite effects.
These effects are known by direct experience.
3
Apply the principle to the observation
If the soul were identical to the body or a part of it, then the same thing (the soul-body entity) would be both perfected and degraded by the same cause, violating the principle. Therefore, the soul must be distinct.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this proof is powerful: it relies on empirical evidence and a logical axiom, showing the soul's immateriality without referring to scripture.
This proof is a cornerstone of rationalist theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة في إثبات هذا المطلوب : إن بديهة العقل قاضية بأن الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد يمتنع أن يكون سبباً لكماله ، وسبباً لنقصانه . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن المواظبة على الأفكار الكثيرة في العلوم الدقيقة توجب كمال حال النفس . فإن النفس بسبب هذه الأفكار تنتقل من القوة إلى الفعل في الكمالات العقلية ، وتصير نورانية مشرقة مضيئة مبتهجة . والمواظبة على الأفكار الكثيرة المذكورة توجب نقصان حال البدن وهلاكه ، وذلك لأن عند حصول الاشتغال بتلك الأفكار والاستغراق فيها ، يستولي الجفاف على الدماغ، ويضعف حال الهضم فيسوء حال البدن . فثبت : أن المواظبة على الأفكار المذكورة ، توجب كمال حال النفس ، ونقصان حال البدن . فلو كانت النفس عين البدن أو جزءاً من أجزائه ، لزم كون الشيء الواحد بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ، سبباً للكمال والنقصان معاً ، وذلك محال.
The eighth proof in establishing this desired [conclusion]: The intuition of reason judges that one thing with respect to one thing cannot be a cause of its perfection and a cause of its deficiency. And when this is established, we say: we know by necessity that persistence in many thoughts in precise sciences brings about perfection of the soul's state. For the soul, through these thoughts, transfers from potentiality to actuality in intellectual perfections, and becomes luminous, shining, radiant, and joyful. And persistence in the aforementioned many thoughts brings about deficiency of the body's state and its destruction, because when one engages in those thoughts and is absorbed in them, dryness overtakes the brain, digestion weakens, and the body's state becomes bad. So it is established: that persistence in the mentioned thoughts brings about perfection of the soul's state and deficiency of the body's state. So if the soul were identical to the body or a part of its parts, then one thing with respect to one thing would be a cause of perfection and deficiency together, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This is the eighth proof for the soul's immateriality, based on the principle that one thing cannot be both the cause of perfection and deficiency for the same other thing. Al-Razi observes that intense intellectual activity (like deep study of precise sciences) perfects the soul: it moves it from potential to actual intellectual perfection, making it luminous and joyful. Simultaneously, such activity harms the body: it dries the brain, impairs digestion, and worsens bodily health. Therefore the same activity causes opposite effects on the soul and body. If the soul were identical to the body or a part of it, then the same thing (the soul-body) would be both perfected and degraded by the same cause, which is contradictory. Hence the soul must be distinct from the body. This argument is a classic kalam proof for the distinction of soul and body.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
كمال
نقصان
سبب
نفس
بدن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul and body are the same substance but have different aspects.
Correction: Al-Razi shows they have contrary effects (perfection vs. deficiency) that cannot inhere in one subject, so they must be distinct substances.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the eighth proof?
A) The soul is a body.
B) One thing cannot cause both perfection and deficiency for the same thing.
C) Intense thinking always harms the body.
D) The soul is potential.

Key Takeaway
The same activity's opposite effects on soul and body prove they are distinct substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in deep learning or reflection, do you notice a contrast between mental exhilaration and physical fatigue? That very experience is proof that your soul is not your body. How seriously do you take the health of your soul compared to your body? Will you commit to feeding your soul with knowledge even when your body complains?
?
Al-Razi says the soul becomes 'luminous, shining, radiant, and joyful' through intellectual perfection. When was the last time you felt that spiritual joy from learning something profound? If it’s been a while, what excuses are you making? What is one specific step you can take today to pursue that joy?
?
If the soul is a distinct substance perfected by knowledge, then your deepest happiness is tied to seeking truth. What prevents you from prioritizing that? Are you willing to sacrifice some bodily comfort tonight to read something that lifts your soul? If not, what does that say about what you truly believe?

The Second Proof: Opposite Effects on Body and Soul
Second proof: opposition of bodily pleasures and spiritual discipline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opposite effects of bodily indulgence and spiritual discipline on the soul and body demonstrate their distinct substances.

Core Idea
What harms the body benefits the soul, proving they are not the same.

Having established the first argument based on the rational power, the author now presents a second, experiential proof. By observing the effects of physical pleasures versus ascetic discipline, we see that what strengthens the soul weakens the body and vice versa. This stark opposition points to two distinct realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's logical progression from observation to conclusion.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author contrasts two situations: one of indulgence and one of discipline. Note how each affects the rational power and the body.
3
Understand the logical inference
The author argues that the opposite effects imply two different substances. Reflect on why this reasoning works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن الاعتبار المذكور يختص بالقوة النظرية ، وهو أيضاً قائم في رياضة الإنسان إذا كان موظباً على الأكل والشرب والتفرج والتنزه بالجسمانيات ، فإنه تختل قوته العقلية ، وتفسد ، ولا يبقى معه منها شيء يلتفت إليه ، أو يعتبر به فإن من المشهور في المثل : البطنة تذهب بالفطنة ، أما إذا راض الإنسان نفسه بالامتناع عن المأكولات ، والانقطاع عن اللذات الجسمانية ، وجلس في بيت مظلم بحيث لا يبصر شيئاً ولا يسمع شيئاً ، وتكلف إزالة الصور المحسوسة والمثل الجسمانية عن الفكر والخيال ، وواظب على هذه الحالة مدة مديدة فإنه تصير نفسه في غاية الكمال ، وبدنه في غاية الضعف والنحافة . وهذا الاعتبار يدل على أن كل ما كان سبباً لسعادة النفس ، فهو بعينه يصير سبباً لشقاوة البدن . وكل ما كان سبباً لشقاوة النفس فهو بعينه سبب لسعادة البدن . وذلك يدل على أن النفس شيء غير البدن ، وغير أجزاء هذا البدن وأعضائه.
وههنا وجه ثالث يناسب الوجهين الأولين ، وهو أن النوم بالنسبة إلى الجسد كالموت وبالنسبة إلى النفس كالحياة وكاليقظة. وذلك لأن الإنسان في حال اليقظة ، تكون نفسه كالنائمة عن عالم الغيب ، الغافلة عن أحوال ذلك العالم ، المعرضة عنها ، فإذا نام الإنسان بحسب جسده، فحينئذ تحصل اليقظة للنفس فتتصل بعالم الغيب ، وتقف على بعض أحوال ذلك العالم ، وهو المنامات الصادقة ، الدالة على أحوال المغيبات ، فإذا استيقظت فقد حصلت اليقظة للبدن ، وصارت النفس نائمة منقطعة عن عالم الغيب.
فثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : أن حياة النفس ، وسعادتها ، ويقظتها توجب موت البدن وشقاوته وقوته . وأن حياة البدن وسعادته ويقظته ، توجب موت النفس وشقاوتها ونومها . وذلك من أدل الدلائل على أن النفس شيء غير البدن ، وغير كل واحد من أجزائه وأبعاضه وهو المطلوب.
And know: that the mentioned consideration is specific to the theoretical power, and it also occurs in the exercise of a person if he is persistent in eating, drinking, amusement, and recreation through physical things, for then his rational power becomes disordered and corrupted, and nothing of it remains with him that is regarded or considered; for it is famous in the proverb: 'Gluttony takes away intelligence.' But if a person disciplines himself by refraining from foods and abstaining from bodily pleasures, and sits in a dark room such that he sees nothing and hears nothing, and takes upon himself to remove sensory images and bodily likenesses from thought and imagination, and persists in this state for a prolonged period, then his soul becomes in utmost perfection, and his body becomes in utmost weakness and emaciation. And this consideration indicates that everything that is a cause for the happiness of the soul is precisely the same thing that becomes a cause for the misery of the body; and everything that is a cause for the misery of the soul is precisely the same thing that becomes a cause for the happiness of the body. And that indicates that the soul (nafs) is something other than the body (badan), and other than the parts of this body and its limbs.
And here is a third aspect (wajh) suitable to the two previous aspects, which is that sleep, with respect to the body, is like death, and with respect to the soul is like life and wakefulness. That is because a person, in the state of wakefulness, his soul is as if asleep from the world of the unseen (ghayb), heedless of the states of that world, turning away from them. So when a person sleeps with respect to his body, then wakefulness occurs for the soul, and it connects to the world of the unseen, and comes to know some of the states of that world—and that is true dreams (al-manamat al-sadiqa) that indicate affairs of the unseen. Then when one awakens, wakefulness occurs for the body, and the soul becomes asleep, cut off from the world of the unseen.
So it is established by these three aspects: that the life of the soul, its happiness, and its wakefulness necessitate the death of the body, its misery, and its [؟] strength; and that the life of the body, its happiness, and its wakefulness necessitate the death of the soul, its misery, and its sleep. And that is among the strongest proofs that the soul is something other than the body, and other than every one of its parts and limbs—and that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third of three arguments proving that the soul is essentially distinct from the body. The author begins by noting that the first argument (not given here) concerned the rational power. He then gives the second argument: when a person indulges in physical pleasures, his rational power weakens; but when he abstains and engages in spiritual discipline in seclusion, his soul becomes perfect while his body weakens. Thus, what benefits the soul harms the body and vice versa. This shows that the soul is not the same substance as the body. The third argument is about sleep: in wakefulness the soul is veiled from the unseen world, but during sleep the soul becomes aware of the unseen through true dreams; when awake, the body is active but the soul is cut off. Again, the opposite states of body and soul indicate their distinction. The conclusion sums up: life and wakefulness of the soul cause death and misery of the body, and vice versa, proving they are distinct substances.

Key Terms
النفس (nafs)
البدن (badan)
القوة النظرية (theoretical power)
رياضة (discipline)
المثل (proverb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the soul and body are one substance because they interact.
Correction: The author shows that their interests are opposed, so they cannot be identical.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what happens to a person's rational power when he indulges in physical pleasures?
A) It becomes perfect.
B) It becomes disordered and corrupted.
C) It remains unchanged.
D) It becomes stronger.

Key Takeaway
The opposite effects of bodily indulgence and spiritual discipline on the soul and body prove they are distinct substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose physical comfort over spiritual growth, what does that reveal about your true attachment? The author says one comes at the cost of the other — which side have you been feeding, and can you honestly say you're content with the result?
?
Think of a time you indulged your body and felt your mind dulled afterward — like the proverb 'gluttony takes away intelligence.' If you truly believed that what harms your body benefits your soul, what one discipline would you adopt starting today?
?
The source says that perfecting your soul makes your body weak. What is the weakness you fear most in your own life? Are you willing to accept that weakness for the sake of your soul's strength?

The Third Proof: Sleep as a Window to the Unseen
Third proof: sleep as a state where the soul connects to the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sleep and wakefulness reveal the soul's distinct connection to the unseen world, separate from the body.

Core Idea
Sleep wakes the soul to the unseen while putting the body to rest, proving they are different.

The third argument draws on a familiar human experience: sleep. The author points out that during sleep, when the body is inert, the soul becomes active and gains knowledge of the unseen through true dreams. Conversely, when awake, the body is active but the soul is veiled. This complementary relationship again shows the soul's distinct nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the contrast between body and soul in sleep and wakefulness.
2
Understand the analogy
Sleep is to the body as death, but to the soul as life and wakefulness. Consider why the author makes this comparison.
3
Connect to the overall proof
The conclusion sums up all three proofs. Reflect on how this third argument reinforces the idea that body and soul are opposites.
