The Ninth Proof: The Soul is Not a Body
The author presents a disjunctive argument that the soul cannot be a body by refuting two of three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disjunctive argument that the soul cannot be a body because it cannot be the entire body nor a part inside it.

Core Idea
If the soul were a body, it must be one of three things—all impossible.

The author presents a clear logical argument: if the soul were a body, it would have to be either the whole body, a part inside it, or a separate body. He then refutes the first two possibilities using empirical observation and rational reasoning. This lesson walks through each step of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Read the passage and find the three possibilities listed for the soul if it were a body.
The author states them clearly at the beginning.
2
Examine the first refutation
Focus on the argument against the soul being the entire body. Notice how the author uses the fact that most body parts lack perception.
Think about bones, humors, and limbs.
3
Examine the second refutation
Now look at the argument against the soul being an internal part. Understand why a single body cannot have uniform contact with all body parts.
This argument is cut off but the reasoning is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة في بيان أن النفس ليست جسماً : هو أن نقول : لو كانت النفس العاقلة الفاهمة الفاعلة جسما ، فكان إما أن يكون مجموع هذا البدن أو جزءاً داخلا فيه ، أو جسماً خارجاً عنه . والكل باطل ، فبطل القول تكون النفس جسماً . إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن تكون عبارة عن مجموع هذا البدن ، وذلك لأنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن أكثر أجزاء هذا المجموع خال عن الفهم والإدراك ، فإن العظام لا حس لها ، والأخلاط لا حس لها البتة ، والرباطات أيضاً لا حس لها ، وهذا بحب الأعضاء البسيطة . وأما بحسب الأعضاء المركبة ، فنحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن الرجلين واليدين وكثيراً من الأعضاء الظاهرة والباطنة ، ليست محلاً للعقل والفهم والإدراك . وإذا ثبت أن أكثر الأعضاء البسيطة والمركبة خال عن العلم والفهم والإدراك ثبت : أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن مجموع البدن هو هذا العالم القادر المريد الفاعل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون العالم القادر المريد الفاعل جسماً في داخل البدن ، فلان بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، يكون ذلك الجسم فاعلاً بالمماسة ، وفاعلاً بالاعتماد على سائر الأجسام . لكن من المعلوم أنه كل ما كانت الحاجة إلى الآلات أقل، كان ذلك الفعل أسهل . وكل ما كانت الحاجة إلى الآلات أكثر ، كان ذلك الفعل أشق . ولا شك أن ذلك الجسم لا يكون مماساً الجميع أجزاء البدن ، لأن الجسم الواحد يمتنع حصوله في الأماكن الكثيرة دفعة واحدة ، فلزم أن يقال : إن تأثيره في بعض أجزاء البدن أسهل من
The ninth proof (al-hujjah al-tasi'ah) explaining that the soul is not a body: it is that we say: if the rational, understanding, active soul were a body (jism), then it would either be the total of this body, or a part inside it, or a body outside it. All are false, so the statement that the soul is a body is false. We say: it is not permissible that it be an expression for the total of this body, because we know by necessity that most parts of this total are devoid of understanding and perception. For bones have no sense, humors have no sense at all, and ligaments also have no sense—and this regarding simple organs. As for composite organs, we know by necessity that the legs, hands, and many of the external and internal organs are not loci for intellect, understanding, and perception. And when it is established that most simple and composite organs are devoid of knowledge, understanding, and perception, it is established that it cannot be said that the total body is this knowing, powerful, willing, acting entity. And we say: it is not permissible that the knowing, powerful, willing, acting entity be a body inside the body, because on the supposition that it were so, that body would be an agent by direct contact (fa'ilan bi al-mumassa) and an agent by relying on other bodies. But it is known that the less need there is for instruments, the easier the action; and the more need there is for instruments, the more difficult the action. And there is no doubt that that body would not be in direct contact with all parts of the body, because a single body cannot exist in many places at once. So it necessarily follows that its effect on some parts of the body is easier than [on others...]
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth argument for the soul's incorporeality. It begins by stating the disjunctive assumption: if the soul were a body, it would have to be either the entire body, a part inside it, or a separate body outside. The author claims all three are false, then refutes the first two. For the first possibility (the soul is the entire body), he argues by induction: most parts of the body—both simple (bones, humors, ligaments) and composite (legs, hands, etc.)—lack perception, so the whole cannot be the perceiving agent. For the second possibility (the soul is a part inside the body), he argues that such an internal body would act by direct contact and reliance on other bodies, but a single body cannot simultaneously touch all parts of the body; therefore, its influence would be uneven—easier on some parts and harder on others. The argument for the second possibility is cut off at the page break, but the conclusion that this is impossible is implied by the earlier claim that all three possibilities are false.

الحجة التاسعة (the ninth proof)
النفس العاقلة الفاهمة الفاعلة (the rational, understanding, active soul)
جسم (body as substance)
مجموع البدن (total body)
جزء داخل (internal part)
جسم خارج (external body)
فاعل بالمماسة (agent by direct contact)
اعتماد (reliance)
آلات (instruments)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the soul is a subtle body inside the physical body.
Correction: The author shows that a single body cannot interact uniformly with all parts, so the soul cannot be an internal body.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility for the soul if it were a body, according to the ninth proof?
A) It is a separate body outside the human body.
B) It is the total of this body.
C) It is a part inside the body.
D) It is a spiritual substance.

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be a body because neither the entire body nor any part inside it can account for the soul's unified perception and action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your true self is not your body, but an immaterial soul that knows and wills, why do you spend almost all your energy on your body's cravings and appearances while ignoring the health of your soul? What will you change this week to feed your soul?
?
The proof shows that no part of your body controls all parts equally. Yet you often feel controlled by your desires or fears. Which desire is currently dominating your choices, and how can you assert your soul's rational authority over it?
?
You believe your soul is not a body — but do you live as if your worth is tied to your physical strength, beauty, or age? Name one specific way you will remind yourself tomorrow that your true value is in your soul's knowledge and will, not in your body.

intermediate

The Ninth Proof: The Soul is Not a Body
Al-Razi refutes two possibilities of the soul being a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the soul is not a body by refuting the possibilities that it could be the sum of the body or a part inside it.

Core Idea
The soul cannot be a body because its effects are uniform, but no body can have uniform effects across all organs.

This passage presents the ninth proof from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author systematically eliminates the possible ways the soul could be a body: as the entire physical body, as a part inside it, or as a separate body. By showing the impossibility of the first two, he establishes that the soul is not a body at all. Let's follow his logical reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core assumption
The author assumes the soul is a body and then lists the three possible ways it could be present in relation to the body.
The third possibility (body outside) is not refuted here but is implicitly rejected.
2
Examine the first possibility
The author argues that many body parts lack sensation and understanding, so the whole cannot be the soul.
He lists bones, humors, ligaments, and limbs as examples.
3
Examine the second possibility
The author argues that such a part would act by contact and need instruments, leading to unequal effects, but the soul's effect is equal across all organs, so it cannot be a body inside.
This is based on the uniformity of sensory experiences like sight and hearing.
4
Conclude the proof
Since the first two possibilities are false, and the third is also false, the soul is not a body.
The conclusion is that the soul is a non-bodily substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة في بيان أن النفس ليست جسماً : هو أن نقول : لو كانت النفس العاقلة الفاهمة الفاعلة جسما ، فكان إما أن يكون مجموع هذا البدن أو جزءاً داخلا فيه ، أو جسماً خارجاً عنه . والكل باطل ، فبطل القول يكون النفس جسماً . إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن تكون عبارة عن مجموع هذا البدن ، وذلك لأنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن أكثر أجزاء هذا المجموع خال عن الفهم والإدراك ، فإن العظام لا حس لها ، والأخلاط لا حس لها البتة ، والرباطات أيضاً لا حس لها ، وهذا بحسب الأعضاء البسيطة . وأما بحسب الأعضاء المركبة ، فنحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن الرجلين واليدين وكثيراً من الأعضاء الظاهرة والباطنة ، ليست محلاً للعقل والفهم والإدراك . وإذا ثبت أن أكثر الأعضاء البسيطة والمركبة خال عن العلم والفهم والإدراك ثبت : أنه لا يمكن أن يقال : إن مجموع البدن هو هذا العالم القادر المريد الفاعل . وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون العالم القادر المريد الفاعل جسماً في داخل البدن ، فلان بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كذلك ، يكون ذلك الجسم فاعلاً بالمماسة ، وفاعلاً بالاعتماد على سائر الأجسام . لكن من المعلوم أنه كل ما كانت الحاجة إلى الآلات أقل، كان ذلك الفعل أسهل . وكل ما كانت الحاجة إلى الآلات أكثر ، كان ذلك الفعل أشق . ولا شك أن ذلك الجسم لا يكون مماساً لجميع أجزاء البدن ، لأن الجسم الواحد يمتنع حصوله في الأماكن الكثيرة دفعة واحدة ، فلزم أن يقال : إن تأثيره في بعض أجزاء البدن أسهل من تأثيره في سائر الأجزاء. ومعلوم أنه ليس كذلك. ولأن تأثيره في الإبصار بالعين ، مثل تأثيره في السماع بالأذن، فثبت : أن تأثير النفس في استعمال جميع الأعضاء بالسوية . وقد دللنا : على أنه لو كان جسماً داخلاً في البدن ، لما حصلت هذه التسوية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن لا تكون النفس جسماً موجوداً في البدن .
The ninth proof in clarifying that the soul is not a body: is that we say: if the rational, understanding, active soul were a body, then it would either be the sum of this body, or a part inside it, or a body outside it. All are false, so the statement that the soul is a body is false. We say: it is not permissible for it to be an expression of the sum of this body, because we know by necessity that most parts of this sum are devoid of understanding and perception. For bones have no sensation, humors have no sensation at all, ligaments also have no sensation, and this is according to simple organs. As for compound organs, we know by necessity that the two legs, the two hands, and many of the outer and inner organs are not a locus for intellect, understanding, and perception. When it is established that most simple and compound organs are devoid of knowledge, understanding, and perception, it is established that it cannot be said that the sum of the body is this knowing, powerful, willing, active entity. We say: it is not permissible for the knowing, powerful, willing, active entity to be a body inside the body, because if that were the case, that body would act by contact and act by dependence on other bodies. But it is known that whenever the need for instruments is less, that action is easier; and whenever the need for instruments is greater, that action is more difficult. There is no doubt that that body is not in contact with all parts of the body, because one body cannot exist in many places at once, so it would necessitate that its effect on some parts of the body is easier than its effect on the rest of the parts. And it is known that this is not the case, and because its effect in sight by the eye is like its effect in hearing by the ear. So it is established that the soul's effect in using all organs is equal. And we have proven that if it were a body inside the body, this equality would not occur. Therefore, it necessitates that the soul is not a body existing in the body.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof that the soul is not a body. The author begins by assuming the soul is a body and then considers three possibilities: it is the sum of the body, a part inside the body, or a body outside the body. He refutes the first two. For the first, he argues that many parts of the body (bones, humors, ligaments, limbs) lack sensation and understanding, so the whole cannot be the soul. For the second, he argues that a body inside would have to act by contact and would need different instruments, leading to unequal effects; but the soul's effect is uniform across all organs, as seen in equal facility of sight and hearing. Therefore, the soul cannot be a body inside. The third possibility is not refuted here, but the conclusion is that the soul is not a body present in the body.

Key Terms

جسم
بدن
أجزاء
مجموع
فهم
إدراك
مماسة
اعتماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the soul is a physical object located inside the body.
Correction: The author does not deny that the soul has a spatial relationship to the body; he denies that it is a body, because a body inside would produce unequal effects, whereas the soul's effects are uniform.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, if the soul were the sum of the body, why is that impossible?
A) The soul would then be divisible
B) Many body parts lack sensation and understanding
C) The body would then be outside the soul
D) The soul would have no effect

Key Takeaway
The soul is not a body because neither as the sum of the body nor as a part inside can account for the uniform and conscious effects we experience.

?

Does this reality shift how you view your own worth, which is not tied to physical appearance or abilities? What would it mean for you to live as if your soul is the real you, not your body?
?
Al-Razi argued that the soul's effect is uniform across sight, hearing, and all actions. But in your life, are you allowing the soul's influence to be uniform—that is, are you equally mindful of your Lord in every action, or do some areas (like work, relationships, or private thoughts) remain unaffected? What step will you take tonight to invite your soul's guidance into that neglected area?

Refuting the Intermingled Body Theory of the Soul
Al-Razi refutes the objection that the soul is a body intermingled with the body via two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical refutations against the idea that the soul is a body intermingled with the body.

Core Idea
A composite soul cannot explain the unity of the self.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi addresses a common objection: why can't the soul be a body whose parts are interwoven with the body's organs? He provides two compelling arguments—based on the unity of the human subject and the interference of faculties—that refute this materialist view.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening where the objectors propose that the soul is a body interwoven with the body, each part assigned to a specific organ.
Notice the detailed description: parts in eye, ear, heart, brain.
2
Understand the first refutation
The author argues that if the soul were a collection of scattered parts, the human would not be a single entity, contradicting the earlier proof that the human is a single subject of all knowledge and actions.
This refutation uses the principle of the unity of consciousness.
3
Grasp the second refutation
Even if the composite model were accepted, each part would be independent. But in reality, occupying one faculty (like anger) prevents another (like desire), proving the soul is a unified entity.
This is an empirical argument from everyday experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : النفس جسم مشابك للبدن ، فبعض أجزائه مشابك في العين ، وبعضها في الأذن ، وبعضها في القلب وبعضها في الدماغ وبهذا التقدير فيتولى كل جزء من أجزاء النفس ، جزءاً من أجزاء البدن . فنقول : هذا باطل من وجوه الأول : إنه على هذا التقدير يكون الإنسان عبارة عن مجموع أجزاء كثيرة متفرقة في أعضاء مختلفة . ونحن قد دللنا في الفصل المتقدم على أن الإنسان يجب أن يكون شيئاً واحداً موصوفاً بجميع العلوم وبجميع الإدراكات ، وأن يكون هو بعينه فاعلاً لجميع الأفعال . وذلك يبطل هذا القدر . وبجميع الذي ذكرتم الوجه الثاني في الجواب : إن بتقدير أن يكون الأمر كما ذكرتم ، إلا أنه يلزم أن يكون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء مستقلاً بالتصرف في ذلك العضو المخصوص ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون اشتغال بعض تلك الأجزاء بفعله الخاص به ، مانعاً للجزء الآخر من فعله الخاص به . ومعلوم : أن هذا حاصل . فإن الإنسان إذا اشتغل بالغضب ، امتنعت الشهوة عليه ، وإذا اشتغل بالشهوة ، امتنع الغضب عليه وإذا اشتغل بالفكر امتنع كلاهما عليه
If they say: why is it not allowed to say that the soul is a body interwoven with the body, such that some of its parts are interwoven in the eye, some in the ear, some in the heart and some in the brain, and accordingly each part of the soul takes charge of a part of the body? Then we say: this is false in several ways. First: on this assumption, the human being would be a collection of many scattered parts in different organs, and we have already established in the previous chapter that the human being must be a single thing characterized by all sciences and all perceptions, and be the very doer of all actions, and that invalidates this assumption. And with all that you mentioned, the second way in response: even if it were as you say, it would still necessitate that each of those parts is independent in its action in that specific organ, and if that were the case, it would be necessary that occupation of some parts with its specific action does not prevent another part from its specific action. But it is known that this is not the case: when a person is occupied with anger, desire is prevented; when occupied with desire, anger is prevented; and when occupied with thought, both are prevented.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and two refutations. The objectors propose that the soul could be a body intermingled with the body, each part responsible for a different organ. The author responds with two arguments. First, this would make the human being a collection of separate parts, contradicting the earlier proof that the human is a single subject of all knowledge and actions. Second, even if that were accepted, each part would act independently, but in reality, engaging in one faculty (like anger) hinders another (like desire), proving that the soul is a single entity, not a composite of independent parts.

Correction: The soul must be a single unified entity; otherwise, faculties like anger and desire would not interfere with each other.

The Third Refutation: Soul Is Not a Body
Razi refutes the claim that the soul is a body pervading the body and establishes its incorporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument refuting the idea that the soul is a body pervading the body, and you will be able to explain why the soul must be a non-corporeal substance.

Core Idea
The soul cannot be a body—neither pervading nor separate—it is a non-corporeal essence.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a refined logical refutation of the claim that the soul is a body that pervades the body. By systematically eliminating possibilities, he arrives at the conclusion that the soul is a substance abstracted from matter. This careful elimination of alternatives is a hallmark of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Notice the logical structure of eliminating possibilities.
2
Identify the four divisions
Razi enumerates three possibilities for the soul's relation to the body and then concludes with the fourth. Find each division in the text.
The divisions are: pervading body, separate body, and the fourth is the non-corporeal substance.
3
Examine the proof against the pervading body
Focus on the argument that if the soul were a body pervading, all organs would be equally principal, but the heart and brain are stronger, so it is false. Then see how he dismisses the separate body option.
This step is key to understanding the refutation.
4
Understand the conclusion
With the three possibilities invalidated, the only remaining conclusion is that the soul is a non-corporeal substance equally related to all parts.
This is the desired outcome of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثالث في الجواب : إن هذا السؤال إنما يتم لو قلنا : إنه حصل مع كل جزء من أجزاء البدن : جزء من أجزاء النفس . حتى يقال : بأن كل واحد منها يحرك ذلك الجزء من غير احتياج إلى شيء من الآلات ، فحينئذ تكون نسبة النفس إلى جميع الأعمال على السوية. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل وإلا لزم أن تكون جميع الأعضاء متساوية في كونها عضواً واحداً رئيساً ، مستقلاً بقبول قوة الحياة . ومعلوم : أنه ليس كذلك . لأن القلب والدماغ أقوى في تعلق النفس بهما من سائر الأعضاء . فثبت بهذه البيانات : أنه لا يجوز أن تكون النفس جسماً سارياً في البدن . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يقال : النفس جسم مغاير للبدن وغير داخل فيه ، فهذا بعيد عن العقل ، ولم يقل به أحد . ولما بطلت الأقسام الثلاثة بقي القسم الرابع ، وهو أن النفس جوهر مجرد عن الجسمية وعلائقها ، وأن نسبته إلى جميع الأجزاء على السوية ، فلم يكن اشتغاله باستعمال بعض الأجزاء ، أشق عليه من استعمال سائر الأعضاء وهو المطلوب .
The third aspect of the answer: This question is only valid if we say that with every part of the body there is a part of the soul, so that it is said that each one moves that part without needing any instruments, then the soul's relation to all actions is equal. But it is known that this is false; otherwise all organs would have to be equal in being a single principal organ, independent in accepting the power of life. And it is known that this is not so, because the heart and brain are stronger in the soul's attachment to them than the other organs. So it is established by these evidences that it is not permissible for the soul to be a body pervading the body. As for the third division—that it is said the soul is a body distinct from the body and not entering into it—this is far from reason, and no one has said it. When the three divisions were invalidated, the fourth division remained: that the soul is a substance abstracted from corporeality and its attachments, and that its relation to all parts is equal, so that its occupation in using some parts is not more difficult for it than using the other organs—and that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the third of three refutations of the idea that the soul is a body pervading the body. The author first states the premise that if the soul were composed of parts corresponding to bodily parts, each part could move its respective body part without instruments, making the soul's relation to all actions equal. He then argues this is false because if it were true, all organs would be equally principal, but observation shows the heart and brain are more strongly attached to the soul. Thus the soul cannot be a body pervading the body. He then dismisses a third possibility—that the soul is a separate body not inside the body—as irrational and unattested. With three invalidated possibilities, the fourth remains: the soul is a non-corporeal substance (جوهر مجرد) equally related to all parts, making its operation equally effortless across organs. The passage concludes that this is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs)
الجسم (al-jism)
الجوهر المجرد (al-jawhar al-mujarrad)
البدن (al-badan)
سارياً (sāriyan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul could be a physical substance that flows through the body like a fluid.
Correction: Razi shows that if the soul were a body (any body, including fluid), it would lead to contradictions because the heart and brain would be no different from other organs. True relation of soul to body is non-corporeal.

Checkpoint

A) The soul is a body pervading the body.
B) The soul is a distinct body outside the body.
C) The soul is a non-corporeal substance equally related to all body parts.
D) The soul is the heart and brain combined.

Key Takeaway
The soul cannot be a body—either pervading or separate—but is a non-corporeal substance equally related to all parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof that the soul cannot be the body for resurrection to be possible
Proof that if the soul equals the body, resurrection is impossible by rational necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why if the soul were identical to the body, bodily resurrection would be rationally impossible according to Sanusi's argument.

Core Idea
Restoring a non-existent is impossible; soul = body makes resurrection impossible.

This lesson presents a key proof from al-Sanusi's al-Matalib al-Aliyah against those who equate the soul with the body. The argument hinges on a fundamental rational principle: a thing that has completely ceased to exist cannot be brought back identically. By examining an objection and its refutation, Sanusi shows that the specific accidents that constitute a person perish at death, making the identical return impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the provided translation. Notice the structure: a principle, an objection, a refutation, and a conclusion.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the foundational principle
Find the sentence stating that restoring a thing identically after annihilation is impossible by primary intuition. This is the bedrock of the argument.
This principle is referred to as 'i'ādat al-ma'dūm'.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that only accidents perish while material parts remain, so the same man can return. Restate this objection in your own words.
The objection assumes man is merely his material parts.
4
Follow the refutation
Sanusi refutes by showing that man is not mere body but a body with specific accidents (knowledge, power, life). These accidents are part of the quiddity, and they cease at death, making identical return impossible.
Trace the logical steps: parts existed before without being the man; after death parts remain but man is gone; thus man requires accidents.
5
Connect the conclusion
The conclusion directly links to the lesson's theme: if soul=body, resurrection is impossible. Understand how the argument proves the soul must be immaterial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالكلية . والشيء إذا فني وعدم فإعادته بعينه بعد فنائه : محال في بديهة العقل فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الإنسان عبارة عن أجزاء مخصوصة ، فإذا مات الإنسان وتفرقت تلك الأجزاء والأعضاء ، فالزائل إنما هو الصفات والأعراض ، وأما تلك الأجزاء فهي بأعيانها باقية . فلا جرم كان ذلك الإنسان باقياً بعينه . فإذا عادت تلك الأعراض إليه ، فإن هذا الإنسان الموجود في هذه الحالة ، هو عين الإنسان الذي كان موجوداً قبل ذلك . فنقول : هذا باطل ، لأن بتقدير أن يكون الإنسان عبارة عن جسم مخصوص ، فإنه يمتنع أن يقال : ذلك الإنسان عبارة عن مجرد تلك الأجسام ، لأن تلك الأجسام ، قد كانت موجودة قبل أن تركبت واتصفت بتلك الصفات، وما كان هذا الإنسان : موجوداً . وإذا مات ذلك الإنسان بعد حياته ، وتفرقت تلك الأجزاء . فتلك الأجزاء باقية ، مع أن ذلك الإنسان غير باق . فعلمنا : أن بتقدير أن يكون الإنسان جسما ، فإنه لا يكون الإنسان المخصوص عبارة عن مجرد الجسمية ، بل يجب أن يكون عبارة عن جسم موصوف بصفات مخصوصة . وهي : العلم والقدرة والحياة. وإذا كان الإنسان عبارة عن مجموع تلك الذوات مع هذه الصفات ، فعند فناء هذه الصفات ، قد عدم أحد الأجزاء المعتبرة في تحقق هذا الإنسان ، من حيث إنه هذا الإنسان . ولما ثبت بحكم بديهة العقل : أن إعادة المعدوم : محال ، ثبت : أن عود ذلك العرض الذي هو أحد أجزاء ماهية ذلك الإنسان : محال . فيلزم : أن يكون عود ذلك الإنسان بعينه محالاً . فثبت بما ذكرناه : أنه لو كان النفس عبارة عن هذا البدن ، أو عن عضو من أعضاء هذا البدن لكان القول بالبعث والقيامة : باطلاً ، ولكان بقاء النفس بعد موت البدن : باطلا . وهذا برهان قوي في إثبات هذا المطلوب
Completely. And a thing when it perishes and ceases to exist, then its re‑creation identically after its perishing is impossible according to the primary intuition of the intellect. If they say: Why may it not be said that man (al‑insān) is an expression of particular parts, so when man dies and those parts and limbs separate, what ceases are only the qualities (ṣifāt) and accidents (a‘rāḍ), while those parts themselves remain, so necessarily that man remains identically; then when those accidents return to him, this man existing in this state is the very man who existed before. We say: This is false, because supposing that man is an expression of a particular body, it is impossible to say that that man is an expression of merely those bodies, for those bodies already existed before they were composed and characterised by those qualities, and this man was not existing. And when that man died after his life and those parts separated, those parts remain while that man does not remain. Thus we know that, supposing man is a body, the particular man is not an expression of mere bodiliness, but must be an expression of a body qualified by specific qualities, namely: knowledge (al‑‘ilm), power (al‑qudra) and life (al‑ḥayāh). And when man is an expression of the totality of those essences together with these qualities, then upon the annihilation of these qualities, one of the parts considered in the actualisation of this man, insofar as he is this man, has ceased to exist. And since it is established by the ruling of the primary intuition of the intellect that the restoration of the non‑existent (i‘ādat al‑ma‘dūm) is impossible, it is established that the return of that accident which is one of the parts of the quiddity (māhiyya) of that man is impossible. Consequently, the return of that man identically is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that if the soul (al‑nafs) were an expression of this body or of an organ of this body, then the assertion of the resurrection (al‑ba‘th) and the rising (al‑qiyāma) would be false, and the survival of the soul after the death of the body would be false. And this is a strong proof (burhān qawī) in establishing this sought conclusion.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhān) that if the soul were identical to the body, then bodily resurrection would be impossible. The author begins by stating a foundational principle of primary intuition (badīhat al‑‘aql): restoring a thing identically after its complete annihilation is impossible. He then considers an objection: someone might argue that man is merely a collection of particular parts (ajzā’ makhṣūṣa); upon death only accidental qualities perish while the material parts remain, so the same man can return when those qualities are restored. The author refutes this by noting that if man were merely a body, then the particular parts existed before composition without constituting the man. After death, the parts remain but the man is gone, showing that man is not mere body but a body qualified by specific attributes (knowledge, power, life). When those attributes perish, one of the constituents of that particular man ceases to exist. Since restoring a non‑existent thing is impossible, the return of that accident – and thus the return of the identical man – is impossible. Therefore, if the soul were the body or a bodily organ, resurrection would be false. The author concludes that this is a strong proof for the intended conclusion.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
إعادة المعدوم
النفس
البعث
الصفات
الأعراض
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the material parts remain, the same person can be restored.
Correction: The author shows that the person requires specific accidents (knowledge, power, life) that are not identical to the parts, and these accidents cease to exist, making the identical return impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why is the restoration of the identical man impossible if the soul is the body?
A) Because the body completely perishes at death.
B) Because restoration of a non‑existent thing (i‘ādat al‑ma‘dūm) is impossible, and the specific accidents that constitute the man cease to exist.
C) Because the soul is immaterial and cannot be restored.
D) Because the parts of the body are destroyed.

Key Takeaway
This passage uses the principle that restoring a non‑existent is impossible to prove that if the soul were the body, resurrection would be impossible — thus the soul must be immaterial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a proof that if your soul were your body, the resurrection would be impossible. Now ask yourself: Do you truly believe in the resurrection? If you do, how does this proof strengthen or challenge that belief? If you do not, what rational basis do you have for rejecting it? Be honest.
?
Consider the principle that 'restoring a non-existent is impossible.' Have you ever considered the implications of this for your own death? If your body perishes completely, what hope do you have of being raised? Does this proof push you to seek an immaterial soul?
?
The author says that a person's identity includes specific attributes like knowledge, power, and life. When you die, those attributes vanish. What part of 'you' do you expect to survive? If you cannot answer with certainty, what is at stake for you in this question?

Why Man Would Be Inferior to Animals Without Resurrection
Proof that rationality causes pain animals lack, so without afterlife man is worse off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that if there were no resurrection and the soul did not survive death, man would be worse off than animals due to the unique mental suffering caused by reason.

Core Idea
Reason makes man miserable in this world; without an afterlife, we are worse than animals.

This passage presents a powerful psychological argument from the author of Al-Matalib al-‘Aliyah. He argues that if the soul did not survive death, then man—despite his rationality—would be the lowest of creatures. Why? Because reason, which elevates us, also brings unique and unavoidable suffering. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core premise
Read the passage and find the second premise: if resurrection and soul survival are false, then man is the lowest of animals. Notice how the author sets up a conditional argument.
2
Understand how reason causes pain
Examine the details: reason forces reflection on past (regret), present (fear of loss, dissatisfaction, desire for more), and future (anxiety). List each source of mental anguish.
The passage breaks present into three specific ways.
3
Compare with animals
Animals lack reason, so they enjoy bodily pleasures without the bitterness (tanghīṣ) that reason causes. This makes their enjoyment more complete than that of humans in this world.
4
Draw the conclusion
If there were no afterlife, man would suffer more and enjoy less than animals, making him inferior. But we know man is not inferior, so the afterlife must exist.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان المقدمة الثانية : وهي قولنا : لو كان القول بالبعث باطلاً وكان القول ببقاء النفس بعد موت البدن باطلاً، لكان الإنسان أخس من جميع الحيوانات . فالدليل عليه : أن الإنسان إنما يتميز عن سائر الحيوانات بقوة العقل والعقل يوجب الشقاء في اللذات الجسمانية ، ويوجب السعادة في الكمالات العقلية ، المنتفع بها بعد الموت . والدليل عليه : أن العاقل يحمله عقله على التفكر في الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل . أما التفكر في الماضي . فكل ما مضى من الأمور المكروهة . فإذا تذكره ، وقع في قلبه حسرة شديدة ، بسبب حصولها في الماضي . وكل ما مضى من الأحوال الطيبة ، فإذا تذكره وقع في قلبه حسرة شديدة بسبب فوتها وزوالها . وأما التفكر في الأحوال الحاضرة. فهذا إما أن يكون في خير أو شر . فإن كان في الخير ، فالعقل يوجب الغم من وجوه : الأول : إنه يخاف زوالها فيحصل له ألم الخوف في الحال . الثاني : إنه لا راحة إلا وفوقها مرتبة أخرى أعلى منها ، فإذا عرف العقل ذلك ، اشتاق إلى المرتبة القوية ، واستحقر المقدار الحاصل ، فيحصل الغم والألم . الثالث : إن كل ما يحصل ، فإنه يزول الالتذاذ به، وحصوله يقوي الرغبة في الأحوال المستقبلة . فهو يوجب الخوف الشديد ، والفزع التام . لأنه لا يعرف أنها تحصل على وفق المراد ، أو على خلاف المراد . مثبت بما ذكرناه : أن العقل يوجب حصول هذه الراحات الجسمانية ، منغصة مقرونة بالخوف والمكروه وألم القلب . وأما سائر الحيوانات ، فإنها لما كانت خالية عن العقل كان انتفاعها باللذات الجسدانية ، خالياً عن التنغيص الحاصل بسبب العقل . فوجب أن يكون انتفاعها بهذه اللذات الجسدانية أكمل من انتفاع الناس بها.
As for the exposition of the second premise, which is our statement: If the assertion about the resurrection (al‑ba‘th) were false and the assertion about the survival of the soul (al‑nafs) after the death of the body were false, then man would be the lowest of all animals. The proof of that is that man is distinguished from other animals only by the power of reason (al‑‘aql), and reason necessitates misery (shaqā’) in bodily pleasures, and necessitates happiness (sa‘āda) in intellectual perfections (al‑kamālāt al‑‘aqliyya) that are benefited from after death. The proof of that is that the rational person is impelled by his reason to reflect on the past, present, and future. As for reflection on the past: every disliked thing that has passed, when he remembers it, severe regret falls into his heart because it occurred in the past; and every pleasant situation that has passed, when he remembers it, severe regret falls into his heart due to its loss and passing. As for reflection on present circumstances: this is either good or evil. If it is good, reason necessitates grief (ghamm) in several ways: First, he fears its loss, so the pain of fear occurs immediately. Second, there is no comfort except that above it is another, higher station; when reason knows that, it longs for the strong station and belittles the attained amount, so grief and pain occur. Third, every attained thing, the pleasure with it ceases, and its attainment strengthens desire for future circumstances. So it necessitates intense fear and complete terror, because he does not know whether those future circumstances will occur according to desire or contrary to desire. Established by what we have mentioned is that reason necessitates that these bodily comforts occur as bitter, mixed with fear, disliked things, and heartache. As for other animals, since they are devoid of reason, their enjoyment of bodily pleasures is free from the bitterness caused by reason. Therefore, it is necessary that their enjoyment of these bodily pleasures is more complete than the enjoyment of people of them.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise of an argument (the first premise was dealt with in the previous lesson). The author argues that if bodily resurrection and the soul's survival after death were false, then man would be inferior (akhass) to all animals. The proof is that man's distinction is reason (‘aql), which causes misery in bodily pleasures while promising happiness in intellectual perfections only after death. The author then details how reason forces man to reflect on past, present, and future, causing unavoidable mental anguish: regret over past losses, fear of losing present goods, dissatisfaction due to awareness of higher stations, and anxiety about the future. Animals, lacking reason, enjoy bodily pleasures without this bitterness. Therefore, if there were no afterlife, man would be worse off than animals. The argument is structured as a conditional proof: if no resurrection, then man is inferior; but man is not inferior (implied), so resurrection must be true.

Correction: This passage argues that in this worldly life, reason causes significant mental anguish through regret, fear, dissatisfaction, and anxiety, making bodily pleasures bitter.

Checkpoint

A) Because reason makes man forget the good things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it take for you to turn that anxiety into a longing for the afterlife rather than a distraction from it?

?
You have the power of reason that animals lack—but are you using it to accumulate regrets and fears, or to seek the intellectual perfections that will benefit you after death? If you died today, would your reason have led you to happiness or to greater misery?

Proof that the Soul Survives: Intellect's Dual Role
Rational proof for soul's survival via reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the soul's survival after death, based on the intellect's ability to cause misery in worldly pleasures and its true benefit in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Intellect causes misery in this life—its true purpose must be in the next.

This passage presents a powerful argument: if the soul did not survive death, the intellect that distinguishes humans would be a curse, making us worse than animals. The author walks through how the intellect taints bodily pleasures, proving that its real benefit lies beyond. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical
The author starts by stating that if the soul does not survive, man would be the lowest of animals. This is the starting point of the reductio ad absurdum.
The entire argument hinges on showing that this conclusion is false.
2
Examine how intellect causes misery
The intellect forces reflection on past, present, and future, each of which leads to regret, fear, and dissatisfaction. This taints bodily pleasures.
The author enumerates three temporal modes: past, present, future.
3
Compare humans to animals
Animals, lacking intellect, enjoy bodily pleasures without any taint. Therefore, if there is no afterlife, humans are worse off than animals.
This comparison is crucial for the reductio.
4
Conclude the intellect's true purpose
Since we know humans are superior, the intellect must have a benefit after death. This proves that the soul survives and is distinct from the body.
The conclusion follows logically from the falsehood of the initial hypothetical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان القول ببقاء النفس بعد موت البدن باطلاً، لكان الإنسان أخس من جميع الحيوانات. فالدليل عليه: أن الإنسان إنما يتميز عن سائر الحيوانات بقوة العقل والعقل يوجب الشقاء في اللذات الجسمانية، ويوجب السعادة في الكمالات العقلية، المنتفع بها بعد الموت. والدليل عليه: أن العاقل يحمله عقله على التفكر في الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل. أما التفكر في الماضي. فكل ما مضى من الأمور المكروهة. فإذا تذكره، وقع في قلبه حسرة شديدة، بسبب حصولها في الماضي. وكل ما مضى من الأحوال الطيبة، فإذا تذكره وقع في قلبه حسرة شديدة بسبب فوتها وزوالها. وأما التفكر في الأحوال الحاضرة. فهذا إما أن يكون في خير أو شر. فإن كان في الخير، فالعقل يوجب الغم من وجوه: الأول: إنه يخاف زوالها فيحصل له ألم الخوف في الحال. الثاني: إنه لا راحة إلا وفوقها مرتبة أخرى أعلى منها، فإذا عرف العقل ذلك، اشتاق إلى المرتبة القوية، واستحقر المقدار الحاصل، فيحصل الغم والألم. الثالث: إن كل ما يحصل، فإنه يزول الالتذاذ به، وحصوله يقوي الرغبة في الأحوال المستقبلة. فهو يوجب الخوف الشديد، والفزع التام. لأنه لا يعرف أنها تحصل على وفق المراد، أو على خلاف المراد. مثبت بما ذكرناه: أن العقل يوجب حصول هذه الراحات الجسمانية، منغصة مقرونة بالخوف والمكروه وألم القلب. وأما سائر الحيوانات، فإنها لما كانت خالية عن العقل كان انتفاعها باللذات الجسدانية، خالياً عن التنغيص الحاصل بسبب العقل. فوجب أن يكون انتفاعها بهذه اللذات الجسدانية أكمل من انتفاع الناس بها. إذا ثبت هذا، فنقول: إن كون العقل موجباً للكمال والسعادة، إنما يحصل بعد الموت. فإذا كان الغرض هو أن النفس لا تبقى البتة بعد موت البدن، لم يحصل من العقل منفعة فيما بعد الموت، وقد بينا أنه من أقوى الأسباب الموجبة لتنغيص هذه اللذات الجسدانية ولتكديرها، وإبطال الانتفاع بها. وحينئذ يظهر عن هذا أنه لو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن لكان الإنسان أخس الحيوانات وأرذلها، وأدناها قدراً وقيمة. فإن امتيازها عن غيرها، ليس إلا بالعقل الذي لا فائدة فيه. إلا تنقيص الخيرات وتنغيص اللذات والطيبات. ولما شهدت بديهة العقل بأن هذا اللازم باطل، وأن الإنسان أشرف الحيوانات وأعلاها قدراً وقيمة، بسبب حصول هذا العقل له، وظهر أن العقل سبب نقصان الخيرات الجسمانية العاجلة، وجب القطع بأن كمال العقل إنما حصل وجلالته ورفعته، إنما حصل بسبب أنه سبب الحصول الكمالات فيما بعد موت البدن. وهذا يدل على أن النفس غير البدن، وعلى أنها باقية بعد موت البدن
And if the statement of the survival of the soul after the death of the body were false, then man would be the lowest of all animals. The proof for it: that man is distinguished from other animals only by the power of intellect, and the intellect necessitates misery in bodily pleasures, and necessitates happiness in intellectual perfections, which are benefited from after death. And the proof for it: that the rational person is driven by his intellect to reflect on the past, present, and future. As for reflection on the past: everything that has passed of disliked matters — when he recalls it, intense regret falls into his heart because of its occurrence in the past. And everything that has passed of pleasant states — when he recalls it, intense regret falls into his heart because of its loss and disappearance. As for reflection on present states: this is either in good or evil. If it is in good, then the intellect necessitates grief in respects: First: he fears its loss, so the pain of fear occurs to him in the present. Second: there is no comfort except that above it there is another higher rank. So when the intellect knows that, it longs for the strong rank and regards the attained amount as contemptible, so grief and pain occur. Third: everything that is attained, the pleasure with it ceases, and its attainment strengthens desire for future states. So it necessitates intense fear and complete terror, because he does not know whether they will occur in accordance with desire or contrary to desire. Established by what we have mentioned: that the intellect necessitates that these bodily pleasures occur tainted, coupled with fear, disliked things, and heartache. As for other animals, since they are devoid of intellect, their enjoyment of bodily pleasures is free from the taint that occurs due to intellect. So it necessarily follows that their enjoyment of these bodily pleasures is more complete than the enjoyment of people of them. When this is established, we say: the fact that the intellect necessitates perfection and happiness occurs only after death. So if the purpose is that the soul does not survive at all after the death of the body, no benefit from the intellect is obtained after death, and we have shown that it is among the strongest causes that taint these bodily pleasures and spoil them and nullify enjoyment of them. Then it appears from this that if the soul did not survive after the death of the body, man would be the meanest of animals, the vilest, and the lowest in rank and value. For his distinction from others is only by the intellect, which has no benefit except diminishing goods and tainting pleasures and delights. And since the innate reason of the intellect witnessed that this consequent is false, and that man is the noblest of animals and the highest in rank and value because of his possession of this intellect, and it appeared that the intellect is a cause of deficiency of immediate bodily goods, it becomes necessary to conclude that the perfection of the intellect and its majesty and exaltedness occurred only because it is the cause of obtaining perfections after the death of the body. And this indicates that the soul is other than the body, and that it survives after the death of the body.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the survival of the soul after death, structured as a reductio ad absurdum. The author begins by stating the hypothetical: if the soul does not survive, then man would be the lowest of animals. The evidence is that man is distinguished by intellect ('aql). Intellect causes misery in bodily pleasures but happiness in intellectual perfections that are only beneficial after death. To prove that intellect causes misery, he analyzes three temporal modes of reflection: past, present, and future. Reflecting on the past causes regret for both disliked and pleasant things. Reflecting on the present good causes grief in three ways: fear of loss, desire for higher ranks, and the cessation of pleasure leading to anxious longing. Thus, intellect taints bodily pleasures, whereas animals without intellect enjoy them purely. Therefore, if there is no afterlife, the intellect becomes a liability, making humans inferior to animals. Since we know intuitively that humans are superior, the denial of the soul's survival is false. Consequently, the intellect's true benefit must lie in the afterlife, proving that the soul is distinct from the body and survives bodily death.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is simply that bodily pleasures are imperfect, so there must be an afterlife.
Correction: The argument is more precise: the intellect actually makes bodily pleasures painful (through fear, regret, etc.), so without an afterlife, the intellect is a net negative, making humans inferior to animals. The survival of the soul is necessary for the intellect to be beneficial.

Checkpoint

B) Because it desires higher pleasures
C) Both A and B
D) Because it cannot enjoy them

Key Takeaway
The intellect's ability to taint worldly pleasures proves that its true benefit lies in the afterlife, which requires the soul to survive death.

?

?
Your intellect often makes you discontent with what you have, always craving a higher rank or fearing loss. Are you willing to accept that this dissatisfaction is a gift pointing you to eternal perfection, rather than a curse? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that?
?
If you had no hope of an afterlife, would you honestly consider yourself worse off than an animal? How does this realization affect your gratitude for the intellect and your urgency to seek its true purpose beyond this fleeting world?

The Rational Argument for Soul Survival
The author argues for the necessity of soul survival through elimination of possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that if the soul did not survive, human creation would be pointless, based on a three-fold classification of possible purposes.

Core Idea
If creation has a purpose, the soul must survive to fulfill it.

This lesson explores a key rational premise in proving the soul's survival: that human creation cannot be vain. The author, Imam al-Sanusi, presents a logical classification of possible purposes for creation, eliminating all but one — that man is created for eternal good. This reasoning lays the groundwork for the necessity of an afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third premise
Read the passage and locate the statement of the third premise: 'If the soul did not survive, creation would be vain.' Notice that this is a conditional claim the author intends to prove.
2
Understand the three possibilities
The author divides the purpose of human creation into three categories: for good, for harm, or for no purpose. List them and see how each is evaluated.
This is a classic kalam method of division and elimination.
3
Follow the elimination reasoning
See why the second possibility (creation for harm) is rejected: good predominates over evil. Then see why the third (no purpose) is invalid: that state already existed in non-existence, so creation would be superfluous.
The argument assumes a wise Creator.
4
Conclude the necessary purpose
The only remaining possibility is that man is created for good, specifically for Paradise and comfort. This good cannot be fully attained in this short worldly life, implying the need for an afterlife and thus the soul's survival.
The passage cuts off but the conclusion is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان المقدمة الثالثة: وهي قولنا: لو لم تكن النفس باقية بعد موت البدن، لكان خلق الإنسان عبثاً. فالدليل عليه: أن هذا الإنسان إما أن يكون مخلوقاً للخير والمصلحة والراحة، أو يكون مخلوقاً للضرر والبلاء، أو لا يعتبر أحد هذين القسمين. أما القسم الثاني، فهو بعيد لما ثبت: أن الخير غالب، والشر مغلوب. وأما القسم الثالث، فباطل. لأن هذا المعنى، كان حاصلا عند البقاء على العدم الأصلي. فلم يبق إلا أن الحق هو القسم الأول، وهو أنه خلق للفوز بالجنة، والراحة، والحكمة، والمصلحة. فنقول: محل الفوز بتلك
And as for the clarification of the third premise: which is our statement: 'If the soul did not survive after the death of the body, the creation of man would be vain.' The evidence for it: that this man is either created for good, benefit, and comfort, or created for harm and affliction, or neither of these two divisions is considered. As for the second division, it is far-fetched because it is established that good predominates and evil is subdued. As for the third division, it is invalid because this condition was already present when remaining in original non-existence. So nothing remains except that the truth is the first division, which is that he was created for attaining Paradise, comfort, wisdom, and benefit. We say: the place of attaining that...
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the third premise of a larger argument for the soul's survival. It is a classification argument: the author posits three possibilities for the purpose of human creation — for good, for harm, or for no purpose. The second possibility (creation for harm) is dismissed because good predominates over evil in the world. The third (no purpose) is invalid because that state already existed in non-existence prior to creation; adding creation for no purpose would be superfluous. Thus, the only remaining possibility is that man was created for good, specifically for attaining Paradise, comfort, wisdom, and benefit. The author then begins to conclude that this good cannot be fully achieved in this world, implying the need for an afterlife, but the passage cuts off.

الخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a proof for God's existence.
Correction: The argument assumes God exists and is wise; it is about the purpose of creation, not the existence of a creator.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the possibility that man was created for harm rejected?
A) Because harm is impossible from a wise creator
B) Because good predominates and evil is subdued
C) Because man is inherently good
D) Because the soul is immortal

Key Takeaway
Human creation must have a purpose, and since that purpose (eternal good) cannot be fully achieved in this life, the soul must survive death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that you were created for a specific purpose — to attain eternal good — then why do you spend so much of your time on things that have no eternal value? What would change today if you took this argument seriously?
?
This argument says that the only viable purpose for your creation is the pursuit of Paradise and comfort. If that is true, what is one specific activity or habit in your life that does not align with that purpose, and what are you going to do about it starting now?
?
The passage argues that creation for no purpose is invalid because it already existed. Are you living as if your life has a purpose, or are you living as if you were created in vain? Answer honestly: what would it mean for you to fully commit to the purpose of attaining Paradise?

First Proof: The Soul is Distinct from the Body and Survives Death
Author presents a logical syllogism and Quranic evidence for the soul's survival after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and scriptural basis for the soul’s distinction from the body and its survival after death.

Core Idea
The soul is not the body; it remains alive after the body dies.

This passage presents the first proof that the human being (the responsible agent) is distinct from the body and continues after bodily death. The author uses a logical syllogism from the second figure, supported by Quranic verses and traditions about grave reward and punishment. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical syllogism
The author argues that if doubt were allowed about a person's death after the body dies, it would lead to absurdity. Therefore, innate reason shows the body is dead. Revelation says the human is alive. So the human must be distinct from the body.
The syllogism uses the second figure of logic.
2
Examine Quranic evidence
Quranic verses about Pharaoh's people being exposed to the Fire morning and evening show that they are alive and rational after death to experience punishment.
This supports the conclusion that the soul survives.
3
Consider traditions about the grave
Traditions about reward and punishment in the grave further confirm that the responsible agent is alive after death and distinct from the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد موته : ميت ، ولو جاز الشك في موته ، لجاز أن يقال : إن جميع الجمادات أحياء عاقلة ناطقة ، وذلك يوجب القول بالسفسطة وزوال العقل . فثبت بمقتضى بديهة العقل : أن بعد موت البدن ، يكون الجسد ميتاً . والله تعالى نص على أن ذلك الإنسان حي بعد هذا الموت ، وحينئذ نقول : الإنسان ليس يميت بعد موت البدن ، وأما البدن وجميع أجزائه فإنه ميت بعد موته ، ينتج من الشكل الثاني [ أن الإنسان ] شيء مغاير للبدن ، ولجميع أجزائه وأبعاضه وهذا كلام في غاية القوة . وأما تقرير هذا المعنى في حق الأشقياء : فهو قوله تعالى : ( النار يعرضون عليها غدواً وعشياً ) وقوله : ( أغرقوا فأدخلوا نارا ) والمراد : أن شرح تعذيب أولئك الكفار والفساق وتعذيبهم ، لا يتم إلا إذا كانوا أحياء عقلاء يميزون بين العذاب وبين الراحة فهذا يقتضي كون أولئك الكفار والفساق أحياء بعد موت البدن ، وحينئذ يرجع القياس المنعقد من الشكل الثاني . وأيضاً : فالقرآن والأخبار ناطقة بحصول ثواب القبر تارة ، وبحصول عذاب القبر . وذلك يدل على أن المكلف حي بعد موت هذا البدن ، وهذا يدل على القطع بأن المكلف شيء مغاير لهذا البدن صوت التقي حياة لا فسنساء لها قدمات قسوم ، وهم في الناس أحياء فالمعنى : أنهم يرزقون الثناء الجميل . أ . هـ وعل رأي القوم هذا : لا يكون نعيم في القبر . ويكون رأي الإمام الرازي غير ملزم إلا لمن يلزم نفسه به .
After its death: dead, and if doubt were allowed concerning his death, it would be allowed to say that all inanimate objects are living, rational, speaking beings, and that would necessitate sophistry and loss of reason. It is established by the dictate of the innate reason that after the death of the body, the corpse is dead. And Allah the Exalted has stated that that human is alive after this death, and then we say: the human is not dead after the death of the body; as for the body and all its parts, it is dead after its death, resulting from the second figure that the human is something other than the body and all its parts and limbs, and this is extremely strong speech. As for the establishment of this meaning regarding the wretched: it is His statement the Exalted: 'The Fire, they are exposed to it morning and evening' and His statement: 'They were drowned and then made to enter a Fire.' And the intended meaning is that the explanation of the torment of those disbelievers and sinners and their punishment is only complete if they are alive, rational, distinguishing between torment and comfort. So this necessitates that those disbelievers and sinners are alive after the death of the body, and then the syllogism formed from the second figure returns. And also: the Qur'an and reports speak of the occurrence of the reward of the grave at times and the occurrence of the punishment of the grave. And that indicates that the responsible agent is alive after the death of this body, and this indicates conclusively that the responsible agent is something other than this body. 'The voice of the pious is a life, not a corruption, they have praises and divisions, and they are alive among the people.' The meaning: they are provided with beautiful praise. End. And according to the opinion of these people, there is no felicity in the grave. And the opinion of Imam al-Razi is not binding except for the one who binds himself to it.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that the human being (the responsible agent) is distinct from the body and continues to exist after bodily death. The author begins with a logical syllogism: If a person could be doubted dead after the body's death, then all inanimate objects could be claimed alive and rational—an absurdity leading to sophistry. Therefore, innate reason dictates the body is dead after death. However, revelation states the human is alive after death. Thus, by the second figure of logic, the human must be something other than the body. The author then supports this with Quranic verses about the punishment of Pharaoh's people, showing they must be alive and rational to experience punishment. Further, traditions about grave reward and punishment prove the soul survives. The passage ends with a poetic line and comment on Imam al-Razi's view, noting that denial of grave felicity is not binding unless one accepts its premises.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
النفس
البدن
الشكل الثاني
عذاب القبر
ثواب القبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The body continues to have life or sensation after death.
Correction: The proof establishes that the body is dead after death; it is the soul that remains alive.

Checkpoint
What is the logical conclusion of the second-figure syllogism presented in the first proof?
A) The body and soul are identical.
B) The human is something other than the body.
C) The body remains alive after death.
D) The soul dies with the body.

Key Takeaway
The human being (the responsible agent) is distinct from the body and continues to live after bodily death, as proven by logic, Quran, and traditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is truly distinct and will outlive your body, why do you invest so much in your physical appearance and so little in your soul's purification? What concrete step can you take today to prioritize your soul?
?
The Qur'an tells us that the wicked are alive after death to experience torment. Do you live with the constant awareness that every sin may be met with punishment immediately in the grave? How would your actions change if you truly believed that?
?
You have just learned a logical proof that the responsible agent (you) is not your body. Does your daily life reflect that belief, or do you act as if only this worldly life matters? Name one decision you will make differently because of this truth.

Second Proof: Universal Human Practice Shows the Soul Survives
Al-Razi argues that universal agreement on praying for the dead proves the soul continues after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal practice of praying for the dead demonstrates, according to al-Razi, that the soul is a separate entity that survives bodily death.

Core Idea
Universal human practice reveals an innate knowledge of the soul's survival.

This proof builds on common human experience. Al-Razi observes that across all cultures and times, people pray for the deceased: blessings for the good, curses for the wicked. This universal practice, he argues, would be meaningless if the person did not continue to exist after death. The fact that all rational people engage in this practice indicates that it is based on a sound rational intuition rather than mere custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm and argument come through.
Notice the two types of responses to the deceased mentioned.
2
Identify the two cases and the central observation
Al-Razi notes two opposite treatments: mercy for the righteous, curse for the wicked. This is the observed datum.
3
Follow the logical argument
If the soul did not survive, these prayers would be absurd. But universal agreement on absurdity is impossible. Therefore, the soul must survive.
The argument relies on the impossibility of universal consensus on falsehood.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The innate dispositions of souls (fitrat al-nufus) and primary rational principles (badā’i‘ al-‘uqūl) bear witness that the human is something other than the body and remains alive after death.
This connects to the broader Islamic theological framework of afterlife and accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : وهي قريبة مما تقدم . إنا نرى جميع طوائف العالم ، وجميع الفرق متفقين على أنه إذا مات إنسان. فإن اعتقدوا فيه أنه كان حال الحياة ، موصوفاً بالصفات المحمودة : ذكروه بالرحمة والتعظيم ، فتارة يقولون : صلوات الله عليه . وتارة يقولون : رحمة الله عليه . وإن اعتقدوا فيه أنه كان [ حال الحياة ] موصوفا بالصفات المذمومة من الجهل والشرور والإيذاء : ذكروه باللعن والخزي والعذاب. ولو قدرنا أن الإنسان لم يبق حياً بعد موت البدن ، لكان هذا الكلام عبثاً باطلا ، فإن من لا يكون حياً ، استحال اتصال الرحمة والروح والراحة إليه ، واستحال أيضاً إيصال الذم واللعن إليه . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكان أهل المشرق والمغرب من الزمان الأقدم إلى هذا الزمان مطبقين على الفعل الباطل [ والقول الفاسد ، وهذا بعيد . فإن إطباق الخلق العظيم على العلم الباطل ] غير ممكن. فهذا يدل على أن فطر النفوس وبدائه العقول شاهدة بأن الإنسان شيء آخر غير هذا البدن ، وأنه يبقى بعد موته حياً ، فإن كان سعيداً كان أهلاً للرحمة ، وإن كان شقياً كان أهلاً للعنة.
The second proof: And it is close to what preceded. We see all sects of the world and all groups agreeing that when a person dies, if they believe that he was, during life, described with praiseworthy attributes, they mention him with mercy and veneration: sometimes they say 'Peace of Allah be upon him', and sometimes they say 'Mercy of Allah be upon him'. And if they believe that he was, during life, described with blameworthy attributes of ignorance, evils, and harm, they mention him with cursing, disgrace, and torment. And if we supposed that the human does not remain alive after the death of the body, this speech would be vain and false, because for one who is not alive, it is impossible for mercy, spirit, and comfort to reach him, and it is also impossible to direct blame and cursing to him. And if it were so, then the people of the East and the West from the most ancient time to this time would be in agreement upon false action and corrupt speech, and this is unlikely; indeed, the agreement of a great creation upon false knowledge is impossible. So this indicates that the innate natures of souls and the primary dictates of intellects bear witness that the human is something other than this body, and that he remains alive after his death: if he is felicitous, he is worthy of mercy, and if he is wretched, he is worthy of curse.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the survival of the soul. It begins by observing that all people, regardless of time or place, consistently pray for the deceased: blessings for the righteous, curses for the wicked. If the person were not alive after death, such prayers would be pointless and absurd. The universal consensus on this practice over millennia cannot be based on falsehood, as it would be impossible for all humanity to agree on something vain. Therefore, the innate disposition of souls and primary rational principles testify that the human is something other than the body and remains alive after death, deserving reward or punishment.

Key Terms
فطر النفوس
بدائه العقول
إطباق الخلق
الرحمة
اللعنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prayers for the dead are merely symbolic or superstitious, with no basis in reality.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that such a universal practice cannot be based on falsehood; it reflects an innate rational recognition that the soul survives death and is capable of receiving benefit or harm.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what does the universal practice of praying for the dead demonstrate?
A) That the body remains conscious after death.
B) That the soul does not survive.
C) That the human is something other than the body and survives death.
D) That prayers have no effect.

Key Takeaway
The universal human practice of praying for the dead is not mere custom but reflects an innate rational recognition that the soul survives the body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray for someone who has died, do you genuinely believe your prayer reaches them? If you doubt that, what does that reveal about your conviction regarding the afterlife?
?
Al-Razi says that all people act as if the soul lives on. But have you ever acted inconsistently with that belief—perhaps grieving as if the deceased were gone forever? What would it take for your actions to fully align with this truth?
?
If you died tonight, what would people say about you? Would they invoke mercy or blame? How does that anticipated judgment weigh on your soul, and what will you do before it's too late?

The Third Proof: Visiting Graves and the Soul's Survival
Author argues that visiting graves for help proves the soul remains active after death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the soul's survival after death, based on the widespread practice of visiting graves and receiving benefit.

Core Idea
People's custom of seeking help at graves shows that souls remain alive and aware after death.

In this passage, the author presents a practical proof for the survival of the soul: the universal human practice of visiting blessed graves, praying, fasting, and supplicating, and experiencing tangible results. He argues that if the dead were completely insentient, such practices would be absurd. This lesson walks through the proof and its supporting examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the argument sink in without over-analyzing.
Notice that the author starts by describing a widespread custom.
2
Identify the core argument and examples
What is the author's main claim? What example does he give? What additional evidence does he provide?
The example of Aristotle's disciples is key.
3
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Consider whether the argument is convincing. The editorial comment suggests some scholars found it weak. Think about why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنه جرت عادة العقلاء بأنهم يذهبون إلى المزارات المتبركة ويصلون ويصومون عندها ، ويدعون الله في بعض المهمات ، فيجدون آثار النفع ظاهرة ، ونتائج القبول لائحة . يحكى أن أصحاب ( أرسطاطاليس ، كانوا كلما صعبت عليهم مسألة ، ذهبوا إلى قبره ، وبحثوا فيها ، فكانت تنكشف لهم تلك المسألة ، وقد يتفق أمثال هذا كثيراً ، عند قبور الأكابر من العلماء والزهاد [ في زماننا ] ولولا أن تلك النفوس باقية بعد موت الأبدان ، وإلا لكانت تلك الاستعانة بالميت الخالي عن الحس والشعور [ عبثا ) ] وذلك باطل . ومما يقوي هذا : هو أن الأحياء قد يصلون لأجل الأموات ، وقد يتصدقون لأجلهم . وقد يتفق كثيراً (5) : أن الحي إذا نام عند قبر بعض الأولياء، يرى في منامه ما يفرج عنه .
The third proof: It is that the custom of rational people is that they go to blessed shrines and pray and fast at them, and supplicate Allah in some important matters, and they find clear traces of benefit and evident results of acceptance. It is narrated that the disciples of Aristotle whenever a problem became difficult for them, they would go to his grave and investigate it, and that problem would be uncovered for them, and many similar things occur at the graves of the great among the scholars and ascetics in our time. Were it not that those souls remain after the death of the bodies, then that seeking help from the dead devoid of sense and feeling would be absurd, and that is false. And what strengthens this is that the living may pray for the sake of the dead, and may give charity for their sake. And it occurs frequently that when a living person sleeps near the grave of some saint, he sees in his sleep what relieves him.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, based on the widespread practice of visiting graves and seeking help from the deceased. Rational people go to blessed tombs, pray, fast, and supplicate, and they experience tangible benefits and answered prayers. The author cites the example of Aristotle's disciples who would visit his grave to solve difficult problems. If souls were not alive and conscious after death, such acts would be futile. Additionally, the living pray and give charity for the dead, and people sometimes have relieving dreams when sleeping near a saint's grave. All this indicates that souls continue to exist and can interact with the living. Note that the editor adds a critical remark questioning this argument, but the author himself presents it as a valid proof.

Key Terms
المزارات المتبركة
أرسطاطاليس
الأكابر
الاستعانة بالميت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the dead are completely unaware and cannot be helped by prayers or benefited by charity.
Correction: The proof argues that if the dead were unaware, seeking help from them would be futile; but since people experience benefit, the dead must be aware.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to support the third proof?
A) The practice of the Companions of the Prophet.
B) The disciples of Aristotle solving problems at his grave.
C) The miracles of Sufi saints.
D) The consensus of all scholars.

Key Takeaway
The universal custom of visiting graves and experiencing benefit is a proof that souls survive bodily death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever visited a grave and felt a sense of connection or relief, even without expecting it? Does that experience challenge your assumptions about what happens after death? Will you now consider it as a sign from Allah?
?
If you knew that a righteous person buried nearby could intercede for you by Allah's permission, would you change how you pray or seek help in your hardest moments? What keeps you from doing so?
?
When you face a problem that seems unsolvable, do you ever turn to those who have passed away, like the disciples of Aristotle did? If not, why—and what would it take for you to humble yourself and seek help from the awliya of Allah, trusting that their souls are alive?
